<p><a href="/wiki/Major-general_(United_Kingdom)" title="Major-general (United Kingdom)">Major General</a> <b>Charles George Gordon</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companion_of_the_Order_of_the_Bath" title="Companion of the Order of the Bath">CB</a> (28 January 1833 – 26 January 1885), also known as <b>Chinese Gordon</b>, <b>Gordon Pasha</b>, and <b>Gordon of Khartoum</b>, was a British Army officer and administrator.</p>
<p>He saw action in the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> as an officer in the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>. But he made his military reputation in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty#Rebellion.2C_unrest_and_external_pressure" title="Qing Dynasty">China</a>, where he was placed in command of the "<a href="/wiki/Ever_Victorious_Army" title="Ever Victorious Army">Ever Victorious Army</a>," a force of Chinese soldiers led by European officers. In the early 1860s, Gordon and his men were instrumental in putting down the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a>, regularly defeating much larger forces. For these accomplishments, he was given the nickname "Chinese Gordon" and honours from both the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor of China</a> and the British.</p>
<p>He entered the service of the <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma'il Pasha">Khedive of Egypt</a> in 1873 (with British government approval) and later became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_governors_of_pre-independence_Sudan" title="List of governors of pre-independence Sudan">Governor-General of the Sudan</a>, where he did much to suppress revolts and the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Sudan" title="Slavery in Sudan">slave trade</a>. Exhausted, he resigned and returned to Europe in 1880.</p>
<p>A serious <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">revolt</a> then broke out in the Sudan, led by a Muslim religious leader and self-proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad</a>. In early 1884 Gordon had been sent to <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> with instructions to secure the evacuation of loyal soldiers and civilians and to depart with them. However, after evacuating about 2,500 British civilians, in defiance of those instructions, he retained a smaller group of soldiers and non-military men. In the buildup to battle, the two leaders corresponded, each attempting to convert the other to his faith, but neither would accede. Besieged by the Mahdi's forces, Gordon organized a city-wide <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Khartoum" title="Siege of Khartoum">defence lasting almost a year</a> that gained him the admiration of the British public, but not of the government, which had wished him not to become entrenched. Only when public pressure to act had become irresistible did the government, with reluctance, send a relief force. It arrived two days after the city had fallen and Gordon had been killed.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gordon was born in <a href="/wiki/Woolwich" title="Woolwich">Woolwich</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, a son of Major General Henry William Gordon (1786–1865) and Elizabeth <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Enderby_Junior" title="Samuel Enderby Junior">(Enderby)</a> Gordon (1792–1873). The men of the Gordon family had been serving as officers in the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> for four generations, and as a son of a general, Gordon was brought up to be the fifth generation; the possibility that Gordon would pursue anything other than a military career seems never to have considered by his parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_p.1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_p.1-1">[1]</a></sup> All of Gordon's brothers also become Army officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_p.1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_p.1-1">[1]</a></sup> Gordon grew up in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Islands" title="Ionian Islands">Ionian Islands</a> (which were under British rule until 1864) as his father was moved from post to post.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> He was educated at Fullands School in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taunton,_Somerset" title="Taunton, Somerset">Taunton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taunton_School" title="Taunton School">Taunton School</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Military_Academy,_Woolwich" title="Royal Military Academy, Woolwich">Royal Military Academy, Woolwich</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GordonDNB_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-GordonDNB-3">[3]</a></sup> In 1843, Gordon was devastated when his favorite sibling, his sister Emily died of tuberculosis, writing years later "humanly speaking it changed my life, it was never the same since."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> After the death of Emily, her place as Gordon's favorite sibling was taken by his very religious older sister Augusta, who nudged her brother towards religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> As a teenager and an army cadet, Gordon was known for his high spirits, a combative streak and tendency to disregard authority and the rules if he felt them to be either stupid and/or unjust, a personality trait that held back his graduation by two years when teachers decided to punish him for flouting the rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> As a cadet, Gordon showed exceptional talents at map-making and in designing fortifications, which led him to choose to become a Royal Engineer or a "sapper".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto29_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto29-7">[7]</a></sup> He was commissioned a <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Engineers" title="Royal Engineers">Royal Engineers</a> on 23 June 1852,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> completing his training at <a href="/wiki/Chatham,_Kent" title="Chatham, Kent">Chatham</a>, and he was promoted to full <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant" title="Lieutenant">lieutenant</a> on 17 February 1854.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The sappers were an elite who performed the "...reconnaissance work, led storming parties, demolished obstacles in assaults, carried out rear-guard actions in retreats and other hazardous tasks."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> As an officer, Gordon showed strong charisma and leadership, but his superiors distrusted him on the account of his tendency to disobey orders if he felt them to be wrong and/or unjust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto29_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto29-7">[7]</a></sup> A man short of stature, but with striking blue eyes, the charismatic Gordon had the ability to inspire men to follow him anywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> Gordon was first assigned to construct fortifications at <a href="/wiki/Milford_Haven" title="Milford Haven">Milford Haven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pembrokeshire" title="Pembrokeshire">Pembrokeshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. During his time in Milford Haven, Gordon was befriended by a young couple, Francis and Anne Drew, who introduced him to evangelical Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_6_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_6-12">[12]</a></sup> Gordon was especially impressed with Philippians 1:21 where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Paul" title="St. Paul">St. Paul</a> wrote: "For to me to live is Christ, and to die is gain", a passage he underlined in his Bible and often quoted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_6_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_6-12">[12]</a></sup> He would attend diverse congregations, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist">Baptist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterian" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a>. Gordon, who once said to a Roman Catholic priest that <i>the church is like the British Army, one army but many regiments</i>, never allied himself to any church or became a member of one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="From_the_Crimea_to_the_Danube">From the Crimea to the Danube</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: From the Crimea to the Danube">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> began, Gordon was assigned to his boyhood home of <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a>, but after several letters to the War Office, he was sent to the Crimea instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Gordon was sent to the Russian Empire, arriving at <a href="/wiki/Balaklava" title="Balaklava">Balaklava</a> in January 1855. Gordon first displayed his death wish as he wrote at the time that he gone "to the Crimea, hoping, without having a hand in it, to be killed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_p.11_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_p.11-15">[15]</a></sup> In the 19th century Russia was Britain archenemy with many people in both nations seeing an ideological conflict between Russian autocracy vs. British democracy, and Gordon was anxious to fight in the Crimea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_p.11_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_p.11-15">[15]</a></sup> Gordon was put to work in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sevastopol_(1854%E2%80%931855)" title="Siege of Sevastopol (1854–1855)">Siege of Sevastopol</a> and took part in the assault of the <a href="/wiki/Redan" title="Redan">Redan</a> from 18 June to 8 September. As a sapper, Gordon had to map out the Russian fortifications at the city-fortress of <a href="/wiki/Sevastopol" title="Sevastopol">Sevastopol</a> designed by the famous Russian military engineer <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Totleben" title="Eduard Totleben">Eduard Totleben</a>, a highly dangerous job that frequently put him under enemy fire, and led him to being wounded for the first time when a Russian sniper put a bullet into him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto44_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto44-16">[16]</a></sup> Gordon spent much time in "the Quarries" as the British called their section of the trenches facing Sevastopol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto44_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto44-16">[16]</a></sup> During his time in the Crimea, Gordon made a number of friendships that were to last for the rest of his life, most notably with <a href="/wiki/Romolo_Gessi" title="Romolo Gessi">Romolo Gessi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garnet_Wolseley" title="Garnet Wolseley">Garnet Wolseley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Graham" title="Gerald Graham">Gerald Graham</a>-all of whom were destined to cross paths with Gordon several times in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto44_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto44-16">[16]</a></sup> On 18 June 1855, the besieging British and French armies began what was intended to be the final assault that would take Sevastopol, which began with a huge bombardment. As a sapper, Gordon was in a front line trench where he was under intense fire, men fell all around him and he was forced to take cover so often that he was literally covered from head to toe with mud, blood and gore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto45_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto45-17">[17]</a></sup> Despite the best efforts of the Allies, the French failed to take the Malakhov fortress while the British failed to take the Redan fortress on 18 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto45_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto45-17">[17]</a></sup> The casualties on the Allied side were quite high that day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto45_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto45-17">[17]</a></sup> Gordon spent thirty-four consecutive days in the trenches around Sevastopol, and earned a reputation as an able and brave young officer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto46_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto46-18">[18]</a></sup> It was said at the British HQ that: "If you want to know what the Russians are up to, send for Charlie Gordon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto46_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto46-18">[18]</a></sup> Gordon took part in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kinburn_(1855)" title="Battle of Kinburn (1855)">expedition to Kinburn</a>, and returned to <a href="/wiki/Sevastopol" title="Sevastopol">Sevastopol</a> at the war's end. During the Crimean war, Gordon picked up an addiction to Turkish cigarettes which was to last for his rest of his life, and many commented that smoking was Gordon's most conspicuous vice as he always seemed to have a cigarette at his lips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> For his services in the Crimea, he received the <a href="/wiki/Crimea_Medal" title="Crimea Medal">Crimean war medal and clasp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GordonDNB_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-GordonDNB-3">[3]</a></sup> For the same services he was appointed a <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Chevalier of the Legion of Honour</a> by the Government of France on 16 July 1856.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1856)" title="Treaty of Paris (1856)">the peace</a>, he was attached to an international commission to mark the new border between the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">Bessarabia</a>. When Gordon first arrived at the city of Galatz (modern <a href="/wiki/Gala%C8%9Bi" title="Galați">Galați</a>) in the Ottoman protectorate of Moldova, he called the city "very dusty and not desirable at all as a place of residence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> As he traveled to Bessarabia, Gordon commented in his letters home about the richness and fertility of the Romanian countryside which produced delicious fruits and vegetables in great abundance and the poverty of the Romanian peasants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> After a visit to Jassy (modern <a href="/wiki/Ia%C8%99i" title="Iași">Iași</a>), Gordon wrote: "The <i>boyers</i> live most of their lives in Paris and society is quite French...The prince keeps a great state and I was introduced to him with much ceremony. The English uniform produces an immediate sensation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Gordon did not speak Romanian, but his fluency in French allowed him to socialize with the Francophile Romanian elite, who were all fluent in French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto47_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto47-24">[24]</a></sup> As the maps that delineated the Russian-Ottoman frontier were all old and inaccurate, Gordon spent much time clashing with his Russian counterparts about where precisely the frontier was and soon discovered that Russians were very keen to have the frontier on the Danube, which Gordon had orders from London to prevent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto47_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto47-24">[24]</a></sup> Gordon called the Romanians the "most fickle and intriguing people on the earth. They ape the French in everything and are full of ceremony, dress, etc...The employees sent by the Moldovan government to take over the ceded territory have been receiving bribes and trafficking in the most disgraceful manner."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Afterwards, Gordon was sent to delineate the frontier between Ottoman Armenia and Russian Armenia, the highlight of which was tobogganing down <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ararat" title="Mount Ararat">Mount Ararat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_18_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_18-26">[26]</a></sup> Gordon continued surveying, marking off the boundary into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. During his time in Armenia, Gordon embraced the new technology of the camera to take what the Canadian historian C. Brad Faught called a series of "evocative photographs" of the people and landscape of Armenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_18_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_18-26">[26]</a></sup> Throughout his life, Gordon was always a keen amateur photographer and was elected a fellow of the Royal Geographical Society to honor him for his Armenian photographs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Gordon returned to Britain in late 1858, and was appointed as an instructor at Chatham. He was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(British_Army_and_Royal_Marines)" title="Captain (British Army and Royal Marines)">captain</a> on 1 April 1859.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:149px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Gordon_Celestiale_Titu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="237" data-file-width="147" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Charles_Gordon_Celestiale_Titu.jpg" width="147"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Gordon_Celestiale_Titu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Charles Gordon as a <i>tidu</i> (Captain General)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gordon was intensely bored with garrison duty in Chatham and often wrote to the War Office, begging them to send him anywhere in the world where British arms were seeing action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1860 Gordon volunteered to serve in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Chinese_Empire" title="Late Chinese Empire">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> When Gordon arrived at <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, he was disappointed to learn he was "just too late for the fighting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto48_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto48-31">[31]</a></sup> Gordon had heard of the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> long before he had set sail for China, and he was at first sympathetic towards the Taipings, led by the charismatic madman Hong Xiuquan who proclaimed himself to be the younger brother of Jesus Christ, viewing them as somewhat eccentric Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto48_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto48-31">[31]</a></sup> But after stopping by at Shanghai, Gordon had visited the Chinese countryside and was appalled at the atrocities committed by the Taipings against the local peasants, writing to his family he would love to smash this "cruel" army with its "desolating presence" that killed without mercy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto48_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto48-31">[31]</a></sup> He arrived at <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a> in September 1860. He was present at the occupation of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> and at the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Summer_Palace" title="Old Summer Palace">Summer Palace</a>. Gordon agreed with Lord Elgin that after the Chinese had killed a group of British and French officers travelling under a white flag to parley that a reprisal was in order, but called the burning of the Summer Palace "vandal-like" and told his sister in a letter "it made one's heart sore" to incinerate the beautiful Summer Palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The British forces occupied northern China until April 1862, then under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_William_Dunbar_Staveley" title="Charles William Dunbar Staveley">General Charles William Dunbar Staveley</a>, withdrew to Shanghai to protect the European settlement from the rebel <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping</a> army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the successes in the 1850s in the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_China" title="Provinces of China">provinces</a> of <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>, and the capture of <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> in 1853 the rebel advance had slowed. For some years, the Taipings gradually advanced eastwards, but eventually they came close enough to Shanghai to alarm the European inhabitants. A militia of Europeans and <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asians</a> was raised for the defence of the city and placed under the command of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Townsend_Ward" title="Frederick Townsend Ward">Frederick Townsend Ward</a>, and occupied the country to the west of Shanghai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plattII_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-plattII-34">[34]</a></sup> The British arrived at a crucial time. Staveley decided to clear the rebels within 30 miles (48 km) of Shanghai in cooperation with Ward and a small French force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plattII_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-plattII-34">[34]</a></sup> Gordon was attached to his staff as engineer officer. <a href="/wiki/Jiading_District" title="Jiading District">Jiading</a>, northwest suburb of present Shanghai, <a href="/wiki/Qingpu_District,_Shanghai" title="Qingpu District, Shanghai">Qingpu</a> and other towns were occupied, and the area was fairly cleared of rebels by the end of 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plattII_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-plattII-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ward was killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cixi" title="Battle of Cixi">Battle of Cixi</a> and his successor <a href="/wiki/Henry_Andres_Burgevine" title="Henry Andres Burgevine">H. A. Burgevine</a>, an American was disliked by the Imperial Chinese authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platt15_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-platt15-35">[35]</a></sup> Burgevine was an unsavory character known for his greed and for being an alcoholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto49_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto49-36">[36]</a></sup> Moreover, Burgevine made little effort to hide his racism, and his relations with the Chinese were very difficult at the best of times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto49_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto49-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>, the governor of the <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> province, requested Staveley to appoint a British officer to command the contingent. Staveley selected Gordon, who had been made a <a href="/wiki/Brevet_(military)" title="Brevet (military)">brevet</a> major in December 1862 and the nomination was approved by the British government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platt15_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-platt15-35">[35]</a></sup> Given Burgevine's alcoholism, open corruption, and a tendency to engage in acts of mindless violence when drunk, the Chinese wanted "a man of good temper, of clean hands and a steady economist" as his replacement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto50_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto50-37">[37]</a></sup> These requirements led Staveley to choose Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto50_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto50-37">[37]</a></sup> Li was impressed with Gordon, writing:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It is a direct blessing from Heaven, the coming of this British Gordon. ... He is superior in manner and bearing to any of the foreigners whom I have come into contact with, and does not show outwardly that conceit which makes most of them repugnant in my sight...What an elixir for a heavy heart-to see this splendid Englishman fight! ... If there is anything that I admire nearly as much as the superb scholarship of Zeng Guofan, it is the military qualities of this fine officer. He is a glorious fellow!...With his many faults, his pride, his temper, and his never-ending demand for money – but he is a noble man, and in spite of all I have said to him or about him, I will ever think most highly of him. ... He is an honest man, but difficult to get on with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Gordon was honest and incorruptible, and unlike many Chinese officers, did not steal the money that was meant to pay his men, but rather insisted on paying the Ever Victorious Army on time and in full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> Gordon's insistence on paying his men meant that he always pressing the Imperial government for money, something which often irritated the mandarins who did not understand why Gordon did not just let his men loot and plunder as a compensation for wages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> Gordon designed the uniform for the Ever Victorious Army, which consisted of black boots together with turbans, jackets and pants that were all green while his personal bodyguard of 300 men wore blue uniforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1863 Gordon took command of the force at <a href="/wiki/Songjiang_Province" title="Songjiang Province">Songjiang</a>, which had received the name of "<a href="/wiki/Ever_Victorious_Army" title="Ever Victorious Army">Ever Victorious Army</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platt15_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-platt15-35">[35]</a></sup> Without waiting to reorganize his troops, Gordon led them at once to the relief of <a href="/wiki/Changshu" title="Changshu">Chansu</a>, a town 40 miles northwest of Shanghai. The relief was successfully accomplished and Gordon quickly won the respect of his troops. Gordon made a point of treating POWs well to encourage the Taipings to surrender and many of his men were former Taipings who chose to enlist in the Ever Victorious Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto50_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto50-37">[37]</a></sup> Unlike Ward and Burgevine, Gordon realized that the network of canals and rivers that divided the Chinese countryside were not obstacles blocking an advance, but were rather "arteries" for allowing an advance as Gordon decided to move his men and supplies via the waterways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Gordon's task was made easier by innovative military ideas Ward had implemented in the Ever Victorious Army. Gordon was quite critical of the way Chinese generals fought the war, observing that the Chinese were willing to inflict and accept gargantuan losses in battle, an approach Gordon disapproved of.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup> Gordon wrote: "The great thing...is to cut off their retreat, and the chances are they will go without trouble; but attack them in the front, and leave their rear open, and they fight most desperately".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup> Gordon always preferred to outflank the Taiping lines rather to take them on frontally, an approach that caused much tension with his counterparts in the Chinese Imperial Army who did not share Gordon's horror at the huge numbers of dead caused by frontal assaults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the morning of 30 May 1863, the Taiping forces guarding the town of Quinsan were astonished to see an armored paddle streamer the <i>Hyson</i> armed with a 32-pounder cannon on the bow, sailing up a canal, at whose prow stood Gordon; following the <i>Hyson</i> was a fleet of 80 junks converted to gunboats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Aboard the <i>Hyson</i> were 350 men from the elite 4th Regiment of the Ever Victorious Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup> Under fire from the Taiping forces, Gordon's men chopped up the wooden stakes the Taipings had placed in the canal, thereby allowing Gordon to outflank the main Taiping defense line and to enter the main canal connecting Quinsan to Suzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup> Gordon's breakthrough caught the rebel army off guard and caused thousands of the enemy to panic and flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup> Gordon disembarked the 4th Regiment with orders to take Quinsan while he sailed up and down the main canal in the <i>Hyson</i>, using the 32-pounder gun to blast apart the Taiping positions on the canal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup> At times, Gordon feared that assaults by the Taiping would take the <i>Hyson</i>, but all the attacks were repulsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The next day, Quinsan fell to the 4th Regiment, which led a proud Gordon to write: "The rebels did not know its importance until they lost it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-44">[44]</a></sup> In its last years, the Taiping movement had oppressed the Chinese peasantry and as the Taipings retreated in the face of fire from the <i>Hyson</i>, Chinese peasants emerged from their homes to cut down and hack to death the fleeing Taipings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-44">[44]</a></sup> After the battle, Gordon was hailed as a liberator from the Taipings by the ordinary Chinese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-44">[44]</a></sup> One British officer serving with the Ever Victorious Army described Gordon at this time as: "a light-built, wiry, middle-sized man, of about thirty two years of age, in the undress uniform of the Royal Engineers. The countenance bore a pleasant frank appearance, eyes light blue with a fearless look in them, hair crisp and inclined to curl, conversation short and decided".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ever Victorious Army was entirely a mercenary force whose only loyalty was to money and whose men were only interested in fighting in order to gain the chance to plunder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-45">[45]</a></sup> Gordon felt very uncomfortable commanding this force and at one point had to order the summary execution one of his officers when the latter tried to take the Ever-Victorious Army over to the Taipings, who had offered a generous bribe for switching sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-45">[45]</a></sup> Gordon had to impose strict discipline on the Ever Victorious Army and worked hard to prevent the Army from engaging in its tendency to loot and mistreat civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> The mercenaries of the Ever Victorious Army, comprising some of the worse social elements of China, Britain and the United States were notorious for their practice whenever they marched into a new district of stealing everything while raping all of the women, which led Gordon to impose harsh discipline, with those soldiers accused of looting and/or rape being taken out and shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_31_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_31-46">[46]</a></sup> Gordon also had the pleasure of defeating Burgevine (a man whom Gordon detested) who had raised a mercenary force and joined the Taipings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_31_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_31-46">[46]</a></sup> After Gordon had surrounded Burgevine's force outside of Soochow, the latter had abandoned his own men and attempted to rejoin the Imperial side, which led Gordon to arrest him and send him to the American consul in Shanghai together with a letter asking that Burgevine be expelled from China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Gordon traveled up and down the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> river valley, he was appalled by the scenes of poverty and suffering he saw, writing in a letter to his sister: "The horrible furtive looks of the wretched inhabitants hovering around one's boats haunts me, and the knowledge of their want of nourishment would sicken anyone; they are like wolves. The dead lie where they fall, and are, in some cases, trodden quite flat by passers by".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-45">[45]</a></sup> The suffering of the Chinese people strengthened Gordon's faith as he argued that there had to be a just, loving God who would one day redeem humanity from all this wretchedness and misery. During his time in China, Gordon was well known and respected by friend and foe alike for leading from the front and going into combat armed only with his <a href="/wiki/Rattan" title="Rattan">rattan</a> cane (Gordon always refused to carry a gun or a sword), a choice of a weapon that almost cost Gordon his life several times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> Gordon's bravery in battle, his string of victories, apparent immunity to bullets and his intense, blazing blue eyes led many Chinese to believe that Gordon had supernatural powers and had harnessed the <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">Qi</a></i> (the mystical life-force traditionally believed in China to govern everything) in some extraordinary way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_41-7"><a href="#cite_note-auto-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon then reorganized his force and advanced against <a href="/wiki/Kunshan" title="Kunshan">Kunshan</a>, which was captured at considerable loss. Gordon then took his force through the country, seizing towns until, with the aid of Imperial troops, the city of <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a> was captured in November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platt15_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-platt15-35">[35]</a></sup> After its surrender, Gordon personally guaranteed that any Taiping rebel who laid down their arms would be humanely treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The Ever-Victorious Army – which was inclined to looting – had been ordered not to enter Suzhou and only Imperial forces entered the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-45">[45]</a></sup> Gordon was thus powerless when the Imperial forces executed all of the Taiping POWs, an act which enraged Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> A furious Gordon wrote that executing POWs was "stupid", writing "if faith had been kept, there would have been no more fighting as every town would have given in".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> In China, the penalty for rebellion was death, and under the Chinese system of familial responsibility all family members of a rebel were all equally guilty even if they had nothing to do with the rebellious individual's acts. The mandarins were thus much inclined to not only execute Taipings, but also their spouses, children, parents and siblings as being all equally guilty of treason. Gordon believed this approach was militarily counterproductive as it encouraged the Taipings to fight to the death, which Gordon felt to be very unwise as the Taiping leader <a href="/wiki/Hong_Xiuquan" title="Hong Xiuquan">Hong Xiuquan</a> had become murderously paranoid, conducing bloody purges of his followers and many Taipings were willing to surrender if only the Imperial government would spare the lives of themselves and their families. Even more importantly, Gordon had given his word of honour that all of the Taipings who surrendered would be well treated, and regarded the massacre as a stain on his honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 January 1864, Gordon was informed that a messenger from the <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">Tongzhi Emperor</a> was coming to see him and he should put on his finest uniform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> When the Emperor's messenger arrived, he had with him servants carrying boxes of silver taels (coins) that numbered 10,000 in total together with banners written in the most eloquent calligraphy celebrating Gordon as a great general and a letter from the Emperor himself written in the best calligraphy on yellow silk thanking Gordon for taking Suzhou and offering all these presents as rewards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> Gordon refused all these gifts and wrote on the Emperor's silk message: "Major Gordon receives the approbation of His Majesty the Emperor with every gratification, but regrets most sincerely that owing to the circumstances which occurred since the capture of Soochow, he is unable to receive any mark of His Majesty the Emperor's recognition".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> The Emperor was much offended when he received Gordon's message at the Forbidden City, and Gordon's military career in China was effectively over for a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> A Scotsman who knew Gordon in China wrote: "he shows the Chinese that if even an able and reliable man, such as he is, is unmanageable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-7"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> Following a dispute with Li over the execution of rebel leaders, Gordon withdrew his force from Suzhou and remained inactive at Kunshan until February 1864.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-platt15_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-platt15-35">[35]</a></sup> Gordon then made a rapprochement with Li and visited him in order to arrange for further operations. The "Ever-Victorious Army" resumed its high tempo advance, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Changzhou" title="Battle of Changzhou">Battle of Changzhou</a>, and culminating in the capture of <a href="/wiki/Changzhou" title="Changzhou">Changzhou Fu</a>, the principal military base of the Taipings in the region. Gordon wrote in his diary: "The HOUR GLASS BROKEN" and predicated that the war would soon be won.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto51_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto51-50">[50]</a></sup> The Ever Victorious Army did not take part in the final offensive that ended the war with the Sack of Nanking as the "Imps" as Gordon called the Imperial Army wanted the honor of taking Nanking, the Taiping capital for themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto51_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto51-50">[50]</a></sup> Instead, the Ever Victorious Army was given the task of taking the secondary cities of Yesing, Liyang and Kitang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto51_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto51-50">[50]</a></sup> At Kitang, Gordon was wounded for a second time on March 21, 1864 when a Taiping soldier shot Gordon in the thigh, but the wound was only slight and Gordon was soon back in action, fighting his last battle at Chang-chou in May 1864.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto51_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto51-50">[50]</a></sup> Gordon then returned to Kunshan and disbanded his army in June 1864.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-8"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> During his time with the Ever Victorious Army, Gordon had won thirty-three battles in succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_49-9"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-49">[49]</a></sup> Gordon wrote a letter home that his losses were "no joke" as 48 out of his 100 officers and about 1, 000 out of 3, 500 soldiers had been killed or wounded in action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Emperor promoted Gordon to the rank of <i>tidu</i> (提督: "Chief commander of Jiangsu province" – a title equal to field marshal), decorated him with the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_yellow_jacket" title="Imperial yellow jacket">imperial yellow jacket</a>, and raised him to Qing's <a href="/wiki/Viscount" title="Viscount">Viscount</a> first class but Gordon declined an additional gift of 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Tael" title="Tael">taels</a> of silver from the imperial treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gmbl_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-gmbl-53">[53]</a></sup> Only forty men were allowed to wear the Yellow Jacket, which was the Emperor's ceremonial bodyguard and it was thus a signal honor for Gordon to be allowed to wear the Yellow Jacket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> promoted Gordon to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lieutenant-colonel" title="Lieutenant-colonel">lieutenant-colonel</a> on 16 February 1864<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and he was appointed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companion_of_the_Order_of_the_Bath" title="Companion of the Order of the Bath">Companion of the Order of the Bath</a> on 9 December 1864.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The traders of Shanghai offered Gordon huge sums of money to thank him for his work commanding the Ever Victorious Army, but Gordon declined all honors of financial gain, writing: "I know I shall leave China as poor as I entered it, but with the knowledge that, through my weak instrumentality, upwards of eighty to one thousand lives have been spared. I want no further satisfaction than this".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup> The British journalist <a href="/wiki/Mark_Urban" title="Mark Urban">Mark Urban</a> wrote: "To those looking at Gordon's actions now, the thanks of companies trading in opium or of a government that killed millions in its suppression of rebellion might seem like the most poisonous endorsements. But at the time people saw a brave man who acted with humanity in an otherwise ghastly conflict, standing out from the other mercenaries, adventurers and cut-throats in wanting almost nothing for himself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup> In a leader (editorial) in August 1884, <i>The Times</i> wrote about Gordon: "the part of the soldier of fortune is in these days very difficult to play with honour...but if ever the actions of a soldier fighting in foreign service ought to be viewed with indulgence, and even with admiration, this exceptional tribute is due to Colonel Gordon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup> The savage Taiping Rebellion – which was the bloodiest war of the entire 19th century taking somewhere between 20 and 30 million lives – is largely forgotten in the West today, but at the time the civil war in China attracted much media attention in the West, and Gordon's command of the Ever Victorious Army received much coverage from British newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup> Gordon also gained the popular nickname "Chinese" Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Gravesend_years">The Gravesend years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Gravesend years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_Charitable_Man">A Charitable Man</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: A Charitable Man">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Gardens,_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Gordon_Gardens%2C_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg/220px-Gordon_Gardens%2C_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Gordon_Gardens%2C_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg/330px-Gordon_Gardens%2C_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Gordon_Gardens%2C_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg/440px-Gordon_Gardens%2C_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Gardens,_Gravesend_-_geograph.org.uk_-_888387.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gordon Gardens, <a href="/wiki/Gravesend" title="Gravesend">Gravesend</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gordon returned to Britain and commanded the Royal Engineers' efforts around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravesend,_Kent" title="Gravesend, Kent">Gravesend</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> to erect forts for the defence of the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>. After he arrived in Britain, Gordon announced to the press that he "did not want to board the tram of the world" and asked to be left alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto52_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto52-57">[57]</a></sup> Following the death of his father he undertook extensive social work in the town including teaching at the local Ragged school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Before 1870, there was no universal school system in Britain, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ragged_Schools" title="Ragged Schools">Ragged Schools</a> were a network of privately funded schools that gave a free education to children whose parents were too poor to afford the school fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto53_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto53-59">[59]</a></sup> The council subsequently acquired the gardens of his official residence, Fort House (now a museum), for the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Gordon's father was against his son working in Chinese service, an estrangement that had not been settled at the time of his death, and Gordon felt immense guilt that his father had died before they were reconciled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto54_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto54-61">[61]</a></sup> Gordon disapproved of the forts he was building at the mouth of Thames to guard against a possible French invasion, regarding them as expensive and useless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto54_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto54-61">[61]</a></sup> When the Duke of Cambridge, the Army's commander visited one of the forts under construction and praised Gordon for his work, he received the reply: "I had nothing to do with it, sir; it was built regardless of my opinion, and, in fact, I entirely disapprove of its arrangement and position".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto54_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto54-61">[61]</a></sup> Besides building forts, Gordon was engulfed in charity and religious fervour during this time. His favorite books were <i>The Imitation of Christ</i> by Thomas á Kempis, <i>Christ Mystical</i> by Joseph Hall and the poem "The Dream of Gerontius" by John Henry Newman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Gordon's closest friends were a couple, Frederick and Octavia Freese, whose son Edward become Gordon's surrogate son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto53_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto53-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his time at Gravesend, Gordon was much involved in charity work, trying to ensure that homeless boys he found begging on the street did not go hungry while attempting to find them homes and jobs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto5_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-63">[63]</a></sup> Many of the "scuttlers" as Gordon fondly called the homeless boys were taken in by him to live at his home, the Fort House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Outside of the Fort House were graffito written on the wall by one of the evidently less educated boys that read: "God Bless the Kernel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto55_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto55-65">[65]</a></sup> Another "scuttler" later recalled: "He made me feel, first of all, the meaning of the phrase, the Goodness of God. Goodness become to me, through Gordon, the most desirable of ideas...We were under the spell of Gordon's personality. We lived in the magic of his mystery-enchanted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto55_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto55-65">[65]</a></sup> Every year, Gordon gave away about 90% of his annual income of £3,000 to charity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> "Chinese Gordon" did not enjoy his celebrity status and though Gordon was extremely charismatic, he only kept a limited circle of friends and found dealing with strangers difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto4_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">[54]</a></sup> A colleague recalled that Gordon's time at Gravesend was the "most peaceful and happy of his life", but Gordon was often bored, and constantly asked the War Office for an assignment to somewhere dangerous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto55_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto55-65">[65]</a></sup> Gordon often spoke nostalgically of his service in China, and wished he could return to that country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality">Sexuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sexuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gordon's charitable work for the boys of Gravesend led to later accusations in the 20th century that he was a homosexual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The <i>Dictionary of National Biography</i> in what is clearly meant to be pun described Gordon as a great "boy lover".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto55_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto55-65">[65]</a></sup> Urban wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is possible that he had sexual feelings for these urchins, but there is no evidence that he ever acted upon them. We can only speculate that his increasing religious devotion may have been an outward manifestation of an internal struggle against sexual temptation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto5_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Gordon never married and is not known to have had a relationship with anyone of the opposite sex (or of the same sex), claiming that his Army service and frequent travels to dangerous places made it impossible for him to marry as he was a "dead man walking" who could only hurt a potential wife as it was inevitable that he would die in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Gordon's parents expected him to marry and were disappointed in his lifelong bachelorhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Urban wrote that the best evidence suggests Gordon was a latent homosexual whose sexual repression led him to funneling his aggression into a military career with a special energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The British historian Denis Judd wrote about Gordon's sexuality:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Like two other great Imperial heroes of his time, Kitchener and Cecil Rhodes, Gordon was a celibate. What this almost certainly meant was that Gordon had unresolved homosexual inclinations which, like Kitchener, but unlike Rhodes, he kept savagely repressed. The repression of Gordon's sexual instincts helped to release a flood of celibate energy which drove him into weird beliefs, eccentric activities, and a sometimes misplaced confidence in his own judgement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The American historian <a href="/wiki/Byron_Farwell" title="Byron Farwell">Byron Farwell</a> in his 1985 book <i>Eminent Victorian Soldiers</i> strongly implied that Gordon was gay, for instance writing of Gordon's "unwholesome" interest in the boys he took in to live with him at the Fort House and his fondness for the company of "handsome" young men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon often said that he wished he had been born a eunuch, which would suggest that he wanted to annihilate all of his sexual desires, indeed his sexuality altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Together with his sister Augusta, Gordon often prayed to be released from their "vile bodies" which their spirits were "imprisoned" in so that that their souls might be joined with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Faught argued that no-one at the time suspected Gordon of having sexual relations with the legions of teenage boys living with him at the Fort House, and the claim he was secretly having sex with the boys of the Fort House was first made by <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Strachey" title="Lytton Strachey">Lytton Strachey</a> in his book <i>Eminent Victorians</i>, which Faught commented may have said more about Strachey than it did about Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto55_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto55-65">[65]</a></sup> Faught maintained that Gordon was a heterosexual whose Christian beliefs led him to maintain his virginity right up to his death as he believed that sexual intercourse was incompatible with his faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught._p_7_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught._p_7-75">[75]</a></sup> About the frequent references in Gordon's letters about his need to resist "temptation" and "subdue the flesh", Faught argued that it was women rather than men who were "tempting" him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught._p_7_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught._p_7-75">[75]</a></sup> The South African minister Dr. Peter Hammond denied that Gordon was a homosexual, citing the numerous statements made by Gordon condemning homosexuality as an abomination, charging that the claim that Gordon was gay was a theory with no foundations in fact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The British historian Paul Mersh suggested that Gordon was not gay, but rather his awkwardness with women was due to <a href="/wiki/Asperger_syndrome" title="Asperger syndrome">Asperger syndrome</a>, which made it extremely difficult for him to express his feelings for women properly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_with_the_Khedive">Service with the Khedive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Service with the Khedive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_the_Danube_to_the_Nile">From the Danube to the Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: From the Danube to the Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 1871, he was appointed British representative on the international commission to maintain the navigation of the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">River Danube</a>, with headquarters at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gala%C5%A3i" title="Galaţi">Galatz</a>. Gordon was bored with the work of the Danube commission, and spent as much time as possible exploring the Romanian countryside whose beauty enchanted Gordon when he was not making visits to <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a> to meet up with his old friend <a href="/wiki/Romolo_Gessi" title="Romolo Gessi">Romolo Gessi</a> who was living there at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> During his second trip to Romania, Gordon insisted on living with ordinary people as he traveled over the countryside, commenting that Romanian peasants "live like animals with no fuel, but reeds", and spent one night at the home of a poor Jewish craftsman whom Gordon praised for his kindness sharing the single bedroom with his host, his wife and their seven children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto52_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto52-57">[57]</a></sup> Gordon seemed pleased by his simple lifestyle, writing in a letter that: "One night, I slept better than I have for a long time, by a fire in a fisherman's hut".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto52_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto52-57">[57]</a></sup> During a visit to Bulgaria, Gordon and Gessi become involved in an incident when a Bulgarian couple told them that their 17-year-old daughter had been abducted into the harem of a pasha, and asked them to free their daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto56_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto56-79">[79]</a></sup> Popular legend has it that Gordon and Gessi broke into the pasha's palace at night to rescue the girl, but the truth is less dramatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto56_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto56-79">[79]</a></sup> Gordon and Gessi demanded that Ahmed Pasha allow them to meet the girl alone, had their request granted after much arm-twisting and then met the girl who ultimately revealed she wanted to go home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto57_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto57-80">[80]</a></sup> Gordon and Gessi threatened to go to the British and Italian press if she was not released at once, a threat that proved sufficient to win the girl her freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto57_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto57-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">colonel</a> on 16 February 1872.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 1872, Gordon was sent to inspect the British military cemeteries in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_Peninsula" title="Crimean Peninsula">Crimea</a>, and when passing through <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> he made the acquaintance of the Prime Minister of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> <a href="/wiki/Raghib_Pasha" title="Raghib Pasha">Raghib Pasha</a>, who opened negotiations for Gordon to serve under the <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">Khedive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma'il Pasha">Isma'il Pasha</a>, who was popularly called "Isma'il the Magnificent" on the account of his lavish spending. In 1869, Isma'il spent 2 million Egyptian pounds (the equivalent to $300 million U.S dollars in today's money) just on the party to celebrate the opening of the Suez Canal, in what was described as the party of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In 1873, Gordon received a definite offer from the Khedive, which he accepted with the consent of the British government, and proceeded to Egypt early in 1874. After meeting Gordon in 1874, the Khedive Isma'il had said: "What an extraordinary Englishman! He doesn't want money!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> Gordon was made a <a href="/wiki/Colonel" title="Colonel">colonel</a> in the Egyptian army.</p>
<p>The French-educated Isma'il Pasha greatly admired Europe as the model for excellence in everything, being an especially passionate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italophile" title="Italophile">Italophile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francophile" title="Francophile">Francophile</a>, saying at the beginning of his reign: "My country is no longer in Africa, it is now in Europe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto58_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto58-84">[84]</a></sup> Isma'il was a Muslim who loved Italian wine and French champagne, and many of his more conservative subjects in Egypt and the Sudan felt alienated by a regime that was determined to Westernize the country with little regard for tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto58_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto58-84">[84]</a></sup> The languages of Khedive's court were Turkish and French, not Arabic. The Khedive's great dream was to make Egypt culturally a part of Europe, and he spent huge sums of money attempting to modernize and Westernize Egypt, in the process going very deeply into debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleveland_.26_Bunton.2C_p._97_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland_.26_Bunton.2C_p._97-85">[85]</a></sup> At the beginning of his reign in 1863, Egypt's debt had been 3 million Egyptian pounds and when Isma'il's reign ended in 1879, Egypt's debt had risen to 93 million pounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> During the American Civil War, when the Union blockade had cut off the American South from the world economy, the price of Egyptian cotton, known as "white gold" had skyrocketed as British textile mills turned to Egypt as an alternative source of cotton, causing an economic blossoming of Egypt that ended abruptly in 1865.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cleveland_.26_Bunton.2C_p._97_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland_.26_Bunton.2C_p._97-85">[85]</a></sup> As the attempts of his grandfather, Muhammad Ali the Great to depose the ruling Ottoman family in favor of his own family had failed due to the opposition of Russia and Britain, the imperialistic Ismai'il had turned his attention southwards and was determined to build an Egyptian empire in Africa, planning on subjecting the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Great Lakes region</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> As part of his Westernization programme, Isma'il often hired Westerners to work in his government both in Egypt and in the Sudan. Ismai'il's Chief of General Staff was the American general <a href="/wiki/Charles_Pomeroy_Stone" title="Charles Pomeroy Stone">Charles Pomeroy Stone</a>, and a number of other veterans of the American Civil War were commanding Egyptian troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Urban wrote that most of the Westerners in Egyptian pay were "misfits" who took up Egyptian service because they were unable to get ahead in their own nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup> Typical of the men that Khedive <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma'il Pasha">Isma'il Pasha</a> hired was <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Baker" title="Valentine Baker">Valentine Baker</a>, a British Army officer dishonorably discharged after being convicted of raping a young woman he been asked to chaperon; after he was released from prison, Isma'il hired Baker to work for him in the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto8_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto8-90">[90]</a></sup> John Russell, the son of the famous war corespondent <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Russell" title="William Howard Russell">William Howard Russell</a> was another European recruited to serve on Gordon's staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farwall_p.116_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farwall_p.116-91">[91]</a></sup> The younger Russell was described by his own father as an alcoholic and spendthrift who "was beyond help" as it was always the "same story-idleness, self-indulgence, gambling and constant promises" broken time after time, leading his father to get him a job in the Sudan, where his laziness infuriated Gordon to no end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farwall_p.116_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farwall_p.116-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equatoria:_Building_Egypt.27s_empire_in_the_Great_Lakes_region">Equatoria: Building Egypt's empire in the Great Lakes region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Equatoria: Building Egypt's empire in the Great Lakes region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="515" data-file-width="379" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg/220px-Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg/330px-Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Gordon_Pasha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General Gordon in Egyptian uniform</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Egyptian authorities had been extending their control southwards since the 1820s. Right up to 1914, Egypt was officially a <i><a href="/wiki/Vilayet" title="Vilayet">vilayet</a></i> (province) of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, but after <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohammed Ali</a> become the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W%C4%81li" title="Wāli">vali</a></i> (governor) of Egypt in 1805, Egypt was a <i>de facto</i> independent state where the authority of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Sultan" title="Ottoman Sultan">Ottoman Sultan</a> was more nominal than real. An expedition was sent up the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, under Sir <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Baker" title="Samuel Baker">Samuel Baker</a>, which reached <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> in February 1870 and <a href="/wiki/Gondokoro" title="Gondokoro">Gondokoro</a> in June 1871. Baker met with great difficulties and managed little beyond establishing a few posts along the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. The Khedive asked for Gordon to succeed Baker as governor of <a href="/wiki/Equatoria" title="Equatoria">Equatoria</a> province that comprised much of what is today <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> Isma'il Pasha told Gordon that he wished to expand Equatoria into the rest of Uganda with the ultimate aim of absorbing the entire Great Lakes region of East Africa into the empire that Isma'il wanted to build in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Baker's annual salary as governor of Equatoria had been £10,000 Egyptian pounds (which was about US$1 million in today's money) and Ismail was astonished when Gordon refused that salary, saying that £2, 000/year was more than enough for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto59_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto59-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a short stay in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, Gordon proceeded to Khartoum via Suakin and Berber. In Khartoum Gordon attended a dinner with the Governor-General, Ismail Aiyub Pasha entertained by barely dressed belly dancers whom one of Gordon's officers drunkenly attempted to have sex with, leading to a disgusted Gordon walking out, saying he was shocked that Aiyub allowed these things to happen in his palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Joining Gordon on the journey to Equatoria was his old friend <a href="/wiki/Romolo_Gessi" title="Romolo Gessi">Romolo Gessi</a> and a former U.S. Army officer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Chaill%C3%A9-Long" title="Charles Chaillé-Long">Charles Chaillé-Long</a> who did not get along well with Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto59_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto59-94">[94]</a></sup> From Khartoum, he proceeded up the White Nile to Gondokoro. During his time in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, Gordon was much involved in attempting to suppress the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> while struggling against a corrupt and inefficient Egyptian bureaucracy that had no interest in suppressing the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto10_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto10-96">[96]</a></sup> Gordon soon learned that his superior, the Governor-General of the Sudan, Ismail Aiyub Pasha was deeply involved in the slave trade and doing everything within his power to sabotage Gordon's anti-slavery work by denying him supplies and leaking information to the slavers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Gordon also clashed with Chaillé-Long, whom he accused of working as an informant for Aiyub Pasha and called him to his face a "regular failure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto60_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto60-98">[98]</a></sup> Chaillé-Long in return painted a very unflattering picture of Gordon in his 1884 book <i>The Three Prophets</i>, whom he portrayed as a bully, a raging alcoholic, an incompetent leader and a rank coward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto60_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto60-98">[98]</a></sup> Faught argued that since no one else who knew Gordon in Equatoria described him in these terms and given that Gordon's accusation that Chaillé-Long was a spy for Aiyub Pasha seems to be justified, that Chaillé-Long was engaging in character assassination as an act of revenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto60_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto60-98">[98]</a></sup> Gordon, despite his position as an official in the Ottoman Empire, found the Ottoman-Egyptian system of rule inherently oppressive and cruel, coming into increasing conflict with the very system he was supposed to uphold, later stating about his time in the Sudan: "I taught the natives they had a right to exist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> In the Ottoman Empire, power was exercised via a system of institutionalised corruption where officials looted their provinces via heavy taxes and by demanding kickbacks known as <i><a href="/wiki/Baksheesh" title="Baksheesh">baksheesh</a></i>; some of the money went to Constantinople with the rest pocketed by the officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto10_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto10-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon established a close rapport with the African peoples of Equatoria such as the <a href="/wiki/Nuer_people" title="Nuer people">Nuer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dinka_people" title="Dinka people">Dinka</a>, who had long suffered from the activity of Arab slave traders, and who naturally supported Gordon's efforts to stamp out the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> The peoples of Equatoria had traditionally <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">worshipped spirits present in nature</a>, but were steadily being converted to Christianity by missionaries from Europe and the United States, which further encouraged Gordon in his efforts as governor of Equatoria, who notwithstanding his position working for the Egyptian government saw himself as doing God's work in Equatoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> Gordon was not impressed with the forces of the Egyptian state. The soldiers of the Egyptian Army were <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallahin" title="Fallahin">fallāḥīn</a></i> (peasant) conscripts who were both ill-paid and ill-trained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> The other force for law-and-order were the much feared <a href="/wiki/Bashi-bazouk" title="Bashi-bazouk"><i>bashi-bazouks</i></a>, irregulars who were not paid a salary, but were expected to support themselves by looting. The <i>bashi-bazouks</i> were extremely susceptible to corruption and were notorious for their brutality, especially to non-Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon remained in the Equatoria province until October 1876. Gordon quickly learned that before he could establish stations to crush the slave trade that he would have to first explore the area to find out where the best places for building stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto61_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto61-99">[99]</a></sup> A major problem for Gordon was malaria, which decimated his men, and led him to issue the following order: "Never let the mosquito curtain out of your sight, it is more valuable than your revolver".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto61_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto61-99">[99]</a></sup> The heat greatly affected Gordon as he wrote to his sister Augusta: "This is a horrid climate, I seldom if ever get a good sleep".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto60_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto60-98">[98]</a></sup> Gordon had succeeded in establishing a line of way stations from the Sobat confluence on the White Nile to the frontier of <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, where he proposed to open a route from <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. In 1874 he built the station at <a href="/wiki/Dufile" title="Dufile">Dufile</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_Nile" title="Albert Nile">Albert Nile</a> to reassemble steamers carried there past rapids for the exploration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Albert_(Africa)" title="Lake Albert (Africa)">Lake Albert</a>. Gordon personally explored Lake Albert and the Victorian Nile, pushing on through the thick, humid jungle and steep ravines of Uganda amid heavy rains and vast hordes of insects in the summer of 1876 with an average daily temperature of 95, down to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kyoga" title="Lake Kyoga">Lake Kyoga</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto62_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto62-100">[100]</a></sup> Gordon wrote in his diary: "It is terrible walking...it is simply killing...I am nearly dead".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto62_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto62-100">[100]</a></sup> Besides acting as an administrator and explorer, Gordon had to act as a diplomat, dealing carefully with <a href="/wiki/Muteesa_I_of_Buganda" title="Muteesa I of Buganda">Muteesa I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kabaka_of_Buganda" title="Kabaka of Buganda"><i>kabaka</i></a> (king) of the <a href="/wiki/Buganda" title="Buganda">Buganda</a> who ruled most of what is today southern Uganda, a man who did not welcome the Egyptian expansionism into the Great Lakes region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Gordon's attempts to establish an Egyptian garrison in the Buganda had been stymied by the cunning Mueteesa, who forced the Egyptians to build their fort at his capital of <a href="/wiki/Lubaga" title="Lubaga">Lubaga</a>, making the 140 or so Egyptian soldiers into his virtual hostages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto62_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto62-100">[100]</a></sup> Gordon chose not to meet Mueteesa himself, instead sending his chief medical officer, a German convert to Islam Dr. <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha" title="Emin Pasha">Emin Pasha</a> to negotiate a treaty whereas in exchange for allowing the Egyptians to leave the Buganda, the independence of the kingdom was recognised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
