<p>Moreover, considerable progress was made in the suppression of the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> During this period Gordon grew close to the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_Society" title="Anti-Slavery Society">Anti-Slavery Society</a>, an evangelical Christian group based in London dedicated to ending slavery all over the world, and who regularly celebrated Gordon's efforts to end slavery in the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> Urban wrote that: "Newspaper readers in Bolton or Beaminister had become enraged by stories about chained black children, cruelly abducted, being sold into slave markets...", and Gordon's anti-slavery efforts contributed to his image as a saintly man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> Gordon had come into conflict with the Egyptian governor of Khartoum and Sudan over his efforts to ban slavery. The clash led to Gordon informing the Khedive that he did not wish to return to the Sudan, and he left for London. During his time in London, Gordon was approached by <a href="/wiki/Sir_William_Mackinnon,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir William Mackinnon, 1st Baronet">Sir William Mackinnon</a>, an enterprising Scots ship owner who had gone into partnership with King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leopold_II_of_the_Belgians" title="Leopold II of the Belgians">Leopold II of the Belgians</a> with the aim of creating a chartered company that would conquer central Africa, and wished to employ Gordon as their agent in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Gordon accepted their offer, believing rather naively in Leopold's and Mackinnon's assurances their plans were purely philanthropic and they had no interest in exploiting Africans for profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> But the Khedive Isma'il Pasha wrote to him saying that he had promised to return, and that he expected him to keep his word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Gordon agreed to return to Cairo, and was asked to take the position of Governor-General of the entire Sudan, which he accepted. He thereafter received the honorific rank and title of a <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">pasha</a> in the Ottoman aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor-General_of_the_Sudan">Governor-General of the Sudan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Governor-General of the Sudan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As governor, Gordon faced a variety of challenges. Besides working to end slavery, Gordon carried out a series of reforms such as abolishing torture and public floggings where those opposed to the Egyptian state were flogged with a whip known as the <i>kourbash</i> made of buffalo hide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto11_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto11-108">[108]</a></sup> Gordon was well known for being utterly obstinate, joking that: "The Gordons and the camels are of the same race. Let them take an idea into their heads and nothing will take it out. I have a splendid camel-none like it; it flies along and quite astonishes the Arabs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto11_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto11-108">[108]</a></sup> However, the real reforms that Gordon wanted would change the basic nature of Ottoman-Egyptian rule by replacing a system based on exploitation of the people by the state with one where the state would work for the betterment of the people, and these aims eluded him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto11_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto11-108">[108]</a></sup> Gordon himself was honest and incorruptible, but he was almost alone in possessing these qualities, and the venal and corrupt Egyptian bureaucrats usually ignored his orders when they conflicted with the chance to make money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup> The Europeans whom the Egyptians had hired to work as civil servants in the Sudan were no better and proved to be just as corrupt as the Egyptians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup> The bribes that the slave traders offered for bureaucrats to turn a blind eye to the slave trade had far more effect on the bureaucrats than did any of Gordon's orders to suppress the slave trade, which were simply ignored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup> Licurgo Santoni, an Italian hired by the Egyptian state to run the Sudanese post office wrote about Gordon's time as Governor-General that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>as his exertions were not supported by his subordinates his efforts remained fruitless. This man's activity with the scientific knowledge which he possesses is doubtless able to achieve much, but unfortunately no one backs him up and his orders are badly carried out or altered in such a way as to render them without effect. All the Europeans, with some rare exceptions, whom he has honoured with his confidence have cheated him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Reflecting these realities, Gordon had to undertake much of the administrative work himself, travelling ceaselessly and constantly all over the Sudan via camel in attempts to make the bureaucracy actually obey his orders; something that occurred when he was present, but stopped as soon as he left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> Gordon's reforming zeal made him popular with the ordinary people of the Sudan as one observer noted that whenever he left and entered the Governor's Palace in Khartoum: "Government officials, consular agents and native people awaited him in large numbers. They celebrated H.E's [His Excellency] arrival with an indescribable uproar".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto7_89-7"><a href="#cite_note-auto7-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1870s, European initiatives against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> caused an economic crisis in northern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, precipitating increasing unrest. Relations between Egypt and Abyssinia (later renamed <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>) had become strained due to a dispute over the district of Bogos, and war broke out in 1875. An Egyptian expedition was completely defeated near Gundet. A second and larger expedition under Prince Hassan was sent the following year and was routed at Gura. Matters then remained quiet until March 1877, when Gordon proceeded to <a href="/wiki/Massawa" title="Massawa">Massawa</a>, hoping to make peace with the <a href="/wiki/People_of_Ethiopia" title="People of Ethiopia">Abyssinians</a>. He went up to Bogos and wrote to the king proposing terms. However, he received no reply as the king had gone southwards to fight with the Shoa. Gordon, seeing that the Abyssinian difficulty could wait, proceeded to Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1876, Egypt went bankrupt. A group of European financial commissioners led by <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Baring,_1st_Earl_of_Cromer" title="Evelyn Baring, 1st Earl of Cromer">Evelyn Baring</a> took charge of the Egyptian finances in an attempt to pay off the European banks who had lent so much money to Egypt. With Egypt bankrupt, the money to carry out the reforms Gordon wanted was not there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> With over half of Egypt's income going to pay the 7% interest on the debt worth 81 million Egyptian pounds that Isma'il had run up, the khedive was supportive of Gordon's plans for reform, but unable to do very much as he lacked the money to pay his civil servants and soldiers in Egypt, much less in the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto63_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto63-110">[110]</a></sup> Gordon travelled north to Cairo to meet with Baring and suggest the solution that Egypt suspend its interest payments for several years to allow Isma'il to pay the arrears owned to his soldiers and civil servants, arguing that once the Egyptian government was stabilised, then Egypt could start paying its debts without fear of causing a revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto63_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto63-110">[110]</a></sup> Faught wrote Gordon's plans were "farsighted and humane", but Baring had no interest in Gordon's plans to suspend the interest payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_59_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_59-111">[111]</a></sup> Gordon disliked Baring, writing he had "a pretentious, grand, patronizing way around him. We had a few words together...When oil mixes with water, we will mix together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_59_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_59-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slavery was the basis of the Sudanese economy, and Gordon's attempts to end the slave trade meant taking on very powerful vested interests, most notably <a href="/wiki/Al-Zubayr_Rahma_Mansur" title="Al-Zubayr Rahma Mansur">Rahama Zobeir</a>, known as the "King of the Slavers" as he was the richest and most powerful of all the slave traders in the entire Sudan. An <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurrection</a> had broken out in <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> province led by associates of Zobeir and Gordon went to deal with it. The insurgents were numerous, and he saw that diplomacy had a better chance of success. On 2 September 1877, Gordon clad in the full gold-braided ceremonial blue uniform of the Governor-General of the Sudan and wearing the <i>tarboush</i> (the type of fez reserved for a pasha), accompanied by an interpreter and a few <i>bashi-bazouks</i>, rode unannounced into the enemy camp to discuss the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Gordon carried no weapons except for his rattan cane (through the <i>bashi-bazouks</i> were armed with rifles and swords), but Gordon showed utterly no fear while his interpreter and the <i>bashi-bazouks</i> were visibly nervous as the rebels numbered about 3, 000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto12_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto12-113">[113]</a></sup> Gordon was met by Suleiman Zobeir, the son of Rahama Zobeir and demanded in the name of the Khedive of Egypt that the rebels end their rebellion and accept the authority of their lord and master, telling Zobeir that he would "disarm and break them" if the rebellion did not end at once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto12_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto12-113">[113]</a></sup> Gordon also promised that those rebels who laid down their arms would not be punished and would all be given jobs in the administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> A tense stand-off ensured, and though the rebels could have easily killed Gordon and his party, as Gordon wrote in a letter to his sister that the rebels were all "...dumbfounded at my coming among them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> This bold move proved successful, as one chief then another pledged his loyalty to the Khedive including Suleiman Zobeir himself, though the remainder retreated to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto9_92-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-92">[92]</a></sup> Gordon visited the provinces of Berber and Dongola, and then returned to the Abyssinian frontier, before ending up back in Khartoum in January 1878. Gordon was summoned to Cairo, and arrived in March to be appointed president of a commission. The Khedive Isma'il was deposed in 1879 in favor of his son Tewfik by the Ottoman Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdul-Hamid_II" title="Abdul-Hamid II">Abdul-Hamid II</a> following heavy diplomatic pressure from the British, French and Italian governments after Isma'il had quarrelled with Baring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon returned south and proceeded to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harrar" title="Harrar">Harrar</a>, south of Abyssinia, and, finding the administration in poor standing, dismissed the governor. In 1878, Gordon fired the governor of Equatoria for corruption and replaced him with his former chief medical officer from his time in Equatoria, Dr. <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha" title="Emin Pasha">Emin Pasha</a>, who had earned Gordon's respect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Gordon then returned to Khartoum, and went again into Darfur to suppress the slave traders. His subordinate, <a href="/wiki/Romolo_Gessi" title="Romolo Gessi">Gessi Pasha</a>, fought with great success in the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal" title="Bahr el Ghazal">Bahr-el-Ghazal</a> district in putting an end to the revolt there. In July 1878, Suleiman Zobeir had rebelled again, leading Gordon and his close friend Gessi to take to the field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto64_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto64-116">[116]</a></sup> In March 1879, Gessi had inflicted a sharp defeat on Zobeir even before Gordon had joined him to pursue their old enemy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto64_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto64-116">[116]</a></sup> After several months of chasing Zobeir, Gessi and Gordon met at the village of Shaka in June 1879 when it was agreed that Gessi would continue the hunt while Gordon would return to Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> On 15 July 1879, Gessi finally captured Zobeir together with 250 of his men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_61_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_61-118">[118]</a></sup> As Zobeir had broken his oath to the khedive by rebelling, Gordon had given Gessi orders to execute Zobeir, and so later that day Gessi had Zobeir and his men publicity beheaded as an example of what happened to those who broke their oaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_61_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_61-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon then tried another peace mission to Abyssinia. The matter ended with Gordon's imprisonment and transfer to Massawa. Thence he returned to Cairo and resigned his Sudan appointment. He was exhausted by years of incessant work. Gordon had gone to the Sudan with high hopes that via his iron will and Christian faith he would defeat the Ottoman-Egyptian system of rule, that he would act as a reformer who would change the system from within to make was unjust just and that he would make things better for the ordinary people of the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> Instead, the Ottoman-Egyptian system had defeated him with almost all of Gordon's reforms having failed owing to the venality of the bureaucracy who shared absolutely none of Gordon's moral outrage at slavery and injustice, and Gordon's dreams of making things better for the ordinary people were dissolved in the face of greed and self-interest of others; the system remained the same creaking slow, utterly corrupt and oppressive apparatus trampling down ordinary people that it always been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-7"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> At the end of his Governor-Generalship of the Sudan, Gordon had to admit that he been a failure, an experience of defeat that so shattered him that he had a nervous breakdown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-8"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> As Gordon travelled via Egypt to take the streamer back to Britain, a man who met him in Cairo described Gordon as a broken man who was "rather off his head".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-9"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup> Before Gordon boarded the ship at Alexandria that was to take him home, he sent off a series of long telegrams to various ministers in London full of Biblical verse and quotations that he claimed offered the solution to all of the problems of modern life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto6_83-10"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1880, he recovered for a couple of weeks in the Hotel du Faucon in <a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a>, 3 Rue St Pierre, famous for its views on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Geneva" title="Lake Geneva">Lake Geneva</a> and because several celebrities had stayed there, such as <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Garibaldi" title="Giuseppe Garibaldi">Giuseppe Garibaldi</a>, one of Gordon's heroes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-119">[119]</a></sup> and possibly one of the reasons Gordon had chosen this hotel. In the hotel's restaurant, now a pub called Happy Days, he met another guest from England, the reverend R.H. Barnes, vicar of <a href="/wiki/Heavitree" title="Heavitree">Heavitree</a> near <a href="/wiki/Exeter" title="Exeter">Exeter</a>, who became a good friend. After Gordon's death Barnes co-authored <i><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/charlesgeorgego00browgoog#page/n8/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">Charles George Gordon: A Sketch (1885)</a></i>, which begins with the meeting at the hotel in Lausanne. Gordon never married. The Reverend Reginald Barnes, who knew him well, describes him as "of the middle height, very strongly built."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The intensely religious Gordon had been born into the Church of England, but he never quite trusted the Anglian Church, instead preferring his own personal brand of Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto11_108-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto11-108">[108]</a></sup> In his worn out state, Gordon had some sort of religious rebirth, leading him to write to his sister Augusta: "Through the workings of Christ in my body by His Body and Blood, the medicine worked. Ever since the realization of the sacrament, I have been turned upside down".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The eccentric Gordon was very religious, but he departed from Christian orthodoxy on a number of points. Gordon believed in <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>. In 1877, he wrote in a letter: "This life is only one of a series of lives which our incarnated part has lived. I have little doubt of our having pre-existed; and that also in the time of our pre-existence we were actively employed. So, therefore, I believe in our active employment in a future life, and I like the thought."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Gordon was an ardent Christian cosmologist, who also believed that the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a> was on the island of <a href="/wiki/Praslin" title="Praslin">Praslin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Gordon believed that God's throne from which He governed the universe rested upon the earth, which was further surrounded by the firmament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> Gordon believed in both predestination writing that "I believe that not a worm is picked up by a bird without the direct intervention of God" and free will with humans choosing their own fate, writing "I cannot and do not pretend to reconcile the two".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farwall_p.114_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farwall_p.114-124">[124]</a></sup> These religious beliefs mirrored differing aspects of Gordon's personality as he believed that he could chose his own fate through the force of his personality and a fatalistic streak often ending his letters with D.V (<i>deo volente</i>-Latin for "God Wills It" i.e whatever God wants will be).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farwall_p.114_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farwall_p.114-124">[124]</a></sup> Gordon's very strong religious feelings led him to devote much time and money to charity both at home and abroad and he was well known for sticking Christian tracts onto city walls and to throw them out of a train window.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> The Romanian historian Eric Tappe described Gordon as a man who developed his own "very personal peculiar variety of Protestantism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto52_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto52-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_offers">Other offers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other offers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 2 March 1880, on his way from London to Switzerland, Gordon had visited King Leopold II of Belgium in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> and was invited to take charge of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a>. Leopold tried very hard to convince Gordon to enter his service, not least because Gordon was known to be modest in his salary demands, unlike Leopold's current agent in the Congo, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a>, who received a monthly salary of 300,000 <a href="/wiki/Belgian_franc" title="Belgian franc">Belgian francs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto65_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto65-125">[125]</a></sup> Gordon rejected Leopold's offers, partly because he was still emotionally attached to the Sudan and partly because he disliked the idea of working for Leopold's Congo Association, which was a private company owned by the King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto65_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto65-125">[125]</a></sup> In April, the government of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a> offered him the position of commandant of the Cape local forces, which Gordon declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto66_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto66-126">[126]</a></sup> A deeply depressed Gordon wrote in his letter declining the offer that he knew, for reasons that he refused to explain, that he had only ten years left to live, and he wanted to do something great and grand in his last ten years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto66_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto66-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May, the <a href="/wiki/George_Robinson,_1st_Marquess_of_Ripon" title="George Robinson, 1st Marquess of Ripon">Marquess of Ripon</a>, who had been given the post of <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_India" title="Governor-General of India">Governor-General of India</a>, asked Gordon to go with him as private secretary. Gordon accepted the offer, but shortly after arriving in India, he resigned. In the words of the American historian <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_C._Y._Hsu" title="Immanuel C. Y. Hsu">Immanuel C. Y. Hsu</a>, Gordon was a "man of action" unsuitable for a bureaucratic job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hsu.2C_1964_p._147_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hsu.2C_1964_p._147-127">[127]</a></sup> Gordon found the life of a private secretary to be in his words a "living crucifixion" that was unbearably boring, leading him to resign with intention of going to East Africa centered on <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> to suppress slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hsu.2C_1964_p._147_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hsu.2C_1964_p._147-127">[127]</a></sup> Hardly had he resigned when he was invited to <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Robert_Hart,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Robert Hart, 1st Baronet">Sir Robert Hart</a>, inspector-general of customs in China, saying his services were urgently needed in China as Russia and China were on the verge of war. Gordon was nostalgic for China, and knowing of the Sino-Russian crisis, he saw a chance to do something significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The British diplomat <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Francis_Wade" title="Thomas Francis Wade">Thomas Francis Wade</a> reported "The Chinese government still holds Gordon Pasha in high regard", and were anxious to have him back to fight against Russia if war should break out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> An exchange of telegrams ensued between the War Office in London and Gordon in Bombay about just what exactly he was planning on doing in China, and when Gordon replied that he would find out when he got there, Gordon was ordered to stay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> However, Gordon disobeyed orders and left on the first ship to China, an action that very much angered the Army's commander, the <a href="/wiki/Prince_George,_Duke_of_Cambridge" title="Prince George, Duke of Cambridge">Duke of Cambridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Gordon arrived in Shanghai in July and met <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>, and learned that there was risk of war with Russia. After meeting his old friend, Gordon assured Li that if Russia should attack he would resign his commission in the British Army to take up a commission in the Chinese Army, an action that if taken risked prosecution under the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Enlistment_Act_1870" title="Foreign Enlistment Act 1870">Foreign Enlistments Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Gordon informed the Foreign Office that he willing to renounce his British citizenship and take Chinese citizenship as he would not abandon Li and his other Chinese friends should a Sino-Russian war begin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> Gordon's willingness to renounce his British citizenship in order to fight with China in the event of war did much to raise his prestige in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon proceeded to Beijing and used all his influence to ensure peace. Gordon clashed repeatedly with <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">Prince Chun</a>, the leader of the war party in Beijing who rejected Gordon's advice to seek a compromise solution as Gordon warned that the powerful Russian naval squadron in the Yellow Sea would allow the Russians to land at <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a> and advance on Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto13_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto13-134">[134]</a></sup> At one point during a meeting with the Council of Ministers, an enraged Gordon picked up a Chinese-English dictionary, looked up the word idiocy, and then pointed at the equivalent Chinese word 白痴 with one hand while pointing at the ministers with the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto13_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto13-134">[134]</a></sup> Gordon further advised the Qing court that it was unwise for the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> elite to live apart from and treat the <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> majority as something less than human, warning that this not only weakened China in the present, but would cause a revolution in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> After speaking so bluntly, Gordon was ordered out of the court and Beijing, but was allowed to stay at Tianjin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto14_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto14-136">[136]</a></sup> After meeting with him in Tianjin, Hart described Gordon as "very eccentric" and "spending hours in prayer", writing that: "As much I like and respect him, I must say he is 'not all there'. Whether religion or vanity, or the softening of the brain-I don't know, but he seems to be alternatively arrogant and slavish, vain and humble, in his senses and out of them. It's a great pity!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto14_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto14-136">[136]</a></sup> Wade echoed Hart, writing that Gordon had changed since his last time in China, and was now "unbalanced", being utterly convinced that all of his ideas came from God, making him dangerously unreasonable since Gordon now believed that everything he did was the will of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto14_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto14-136">[136]</a></sup> Gordon was ordered home by London as the Foreign Office was not comfortable with the idea of him commanding the Chinese Army against Russia if war should break out, believing that this would cause an Anglo-Russian war and Gordon was told that he would be dishonorably discharged if he remained in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Though the Qing court rejected Gordon's advice to seek a compromise with Russia in the summer of 1880, Gordon's assessment of China's military backwardness and his stark warnings that the Russians would win if a war did break out played an important role in ultimately strengthening the peace party at the court and preventing war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon returned to Britain and rented an apartment on 8 Victoria Grove in London. In October 1880 Gordon paid a two-week visit to <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, landing at <a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a> and traveling over much of the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Gordon was sickened by the poverty of the Irish farmers, which led him to write a six-page memo to the Prime Minister, William Gladstone, urging land reforms in Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Gordon wrote: "The peasantry of the Northwest and Southwest of Ireland are much worse off than any of the inhabitants of Bulgaria, Asia Minor, China, India or the Sudan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> Having been to all of those places and thus speaking with some authority, Gordon announced the "scandal" of poverty in Ireland could only be ended if the government were to buy the land of the Ascendency families as the Anglo-Irish elite was known and give it to their poor Irish tenant farmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> Gordon compared his plans for rural reform in Ireland to ending slavery in the British Empire in 1833, and ended his letter with the claim that if this was done, the unity of the United Kingdom would be preserved as the Irish would appreciate this great act of justice and the Irish independence movement would cease to exist as "they would have nothing more to seek from agitation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> Somewhat tactlessly, Gordon reminded Gladstone that his father had owned a slave plantation in Jamaica and had been one of those slave-owners compensated by the Crown in 1833 for the freeing of his slaves, a bit of Gladstone family history that the Prime Minister did not like to have discussed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> Besides championing land reform in Ireland, Gordon spent the winter of 1880-81 in London socializing with his family and his few friends such as <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Alfred Tennyson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-4"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 1881 he left for <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> as Commander, Royal Engineers. He remained in Mauritius until March 1882. The American historian <a href="/wiki/John_Semple_Galbraith" title="John Semple Galbraith">John Semple Galbraith</a> described Gordon as suffering from "utter boredom" during his time in the Mauritius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto68_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto68-142">[142]</a></sup> Gordon saw his work in building forts to protect the Mauritius from a possible Russian naval attack as pointless, and his main achievement during his time there was to advise the Crown to turn the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a> islands, whose beauty had greatly moved Gordon, into a new crown colony as Gordon argued it was impossible to govern the Seychelles from <a href="/wiki/Port_Louis" title="Port Louis">Port Louis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-5"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> In a memo to London, Gordon warned against over-reliance on the Suez canal, where the Russians could easily sink one ship to block the entire canal, thus leading Gordon to advise upon improving the Cape route to India with Britain developing a series of bases in Africa and in the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-6"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> Gordon visited the Seychelles in the summer of 1881 and decided the islands were location of the Garden of Eden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faught_69_141-7"><a href="#cite_note-Faught_69-141">[141]</a></sup> On the island of Valle de Mai, Gordon believed that he found the Tree of the Knowledge of Good and Evil in the form of a coco de mer tree that bore a close resemblance to a woman's body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Promoted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_General_(United_Kingdom)" title="Major General (United Kingdom)">major-general</a> on 23 March 1882,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> he was sent to the Cape to aid in settling affairs in <a href="/wiki/Basutoland" title="Basutoland">Basutoland</a>, but he returned to the United Kingdom after only a few months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The principle problem faced by Gordon in South Africa was <a href="/wiki/Basutoland" title="Basutoland">Basutoland</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>), a small kingdom whose ruler, King <a href="/wiki/Letsie_I_Moshoeshoe_of_Lesotho" title="Letsie I Moshoeshoe of Lesotho">Moshoeshoe</a> had decided to have his nation become a British protectorate rather be annexed by the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto69_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto69-146">[146]</a></sup> One of Moshoeshoe's sons, Prince Masupha had rebelled in protest against his father's plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto69_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto69-146">[146]</a></sup> Before settling out on a trip to mediate the end of the civil war in Basutoland, Gordon met with <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a> to seek his advice. Rhodes later recalled he and Gordon got along "capitally together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto69_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto69-146">[146]</a></sup> After arriving in Basutoland, Gordon met with Prince Masupha to persuade him to lay down his arms and to accept his father's protectorate plan rather than have his kingdom annexed to the Orange Free State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto70_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto70-147">[147]</a></sup> Faught wrote that Gordon was only in South Africa for a short time, but one of his enduring legacies is the existence of Lesotho, which otherwise would have been annexed to the Orange Free State and whose people would had thus suffered from apartheid in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto70_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto70-147">[147]</a></sup> After his return from South Africa, Galbraith remarked that Gordon had a "strange combination of melancholia, resignation and mysticism" that he was destined by God both to do something great and to die soon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto68_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto68-142">[142]</a></sup> During this time, Leopold who was most anxious to fire Stanley with his ever-increasing demands for more money, constantly wrote to Gordon asking him to be his agent in the Congo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto68_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto68-142">[142]</a></sup> Gordon had come to distrust Leopold, but he came to believe that perhaps he could do some good in the Congo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto68_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto68-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Being unemployed, Gordon decided to go to <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> which at the time was part of the Ottoman <i>vilayet</i> of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Syria" title="Ottoman Syria">Syria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> a region he had long desired to visit, where he would remain for a year (1882–83). During his "career break" in the Holy Land, the very religious Gordon sought to explore his faith and biblical sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto15_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto15-149">[149]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, Gordon lived with an American lawyer <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Spafford" title="Horatio Spafford">Horatio Spafford</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Anna_Spafford" title="Anna Spafford">Anna Spafford</a>, who were the leaders of the <a href="/wiki/American_Colony,_Jerusalem" title="American Colony, Jerusalem">American Colony</a> in the Holy City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The Spaffords had lost their home and much of their fortune in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Chicago_Fire" title="Great Chicago Fire">Great Chicago Fire</a> and then had seen one of their sons die of scarlet fever, four of their daughters drowned in a shipwreck followed by the death of another son from scarlet fever, causing them to turn to religion as consolation for unbearable tragedy, making them very congenial company for Gordon during his stay in Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> After his visit, Gordon suggested in his book <i>Reflections in Palestine</i> a different location for <a href="/wiki/Calvary" title="Calvary">Golgotha</a>, the site of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucifixion</a>. The site lies north of the traditional site at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Holy Sepulchre</a> and is now known as "The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garden_Tomb" title="Garden Tomb">Garden Tomb</a>," or sometimes as "Gordon's Calvary."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Gordon's interest was prompted by his religious beliefs, as he had become an <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">evangelical Christian</a> in 1854.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>King Leopold II then asked Gordon again to take charge of the Congo Free State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> He accepted and returned to London to make preparations, but soon after his arrival the British requested that he proceed immediately to the Sudan, where the situation had deteriorated badly after his departure—another revolt had arisen, led by the self-proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Mohammed Ahmed</a>. The Mahdi is a messianic figure in Islam which tradition holds will appear at the dawn of every new (Islamic) century to strike down the enemies of Islam. The year 1881 was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_year" title="Islamic year">Islamic year</a> 1299, and to mark the coming of the new century, Ahmed announced that he was the Mahdi, and proclaimed a <i><a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a></i> against the Egyptian state. The long exploitation of the Sudan by Egypt led many Sudanese to rally to the Mahdi's <a href="/wiki/Black_Standard" title="Black Standard">black banner</a> as he promised to expel the Egyptians, whom Ahmed denounced as apostates and he announced he would establish an Islamic fundamentalist state that mark a return to the "pure Islam" said to have been practiced in the days of the Prophet Mohamed in Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Additionally, Baring's policy of raising taxes to pay off the debts Isma'il had run up sparked much resentment in both Egypt and the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> In 1882, nationalist rage in Egypt against Baring's economic policies led to the revolt by Colonel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urabi_Pasha" title="Urabi Pasha">Urabi Pasha</a>, which was put down by British troops. From September 1882 onwards, Egypt was a <i>de facto</i> British protectorate effectively ruled by Baring, through in theory Egypt remained an Ottoman province with a very wide degree of autonomy until 1914. With Egypt under British rule, the British also inherited the problems of Egypt's colony, the Sudan, which the Egyptians were losing control of to the Mahdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mahdist_uprising">Mahdist uprising</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mahdist uprising">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">Mahdist War</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_to_Khartoum">Mission to Khartoum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mission to Khartoum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="724" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg/220px-Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg/330px-Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg/440px-Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad</a>, the self-proclaimed <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Egyptian forces in the Sudan were insufficient to cope with the rebels, and the northern government was occupied in the suppression of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urabi_Revolt" title="Urabi Revolt">Urabi Revolt</a>. By September 1882, the Egyptian position in the Sudan had grown perilous. In September 1883, an Egyptian Army force under Colonel <a href="/wiki/William_Hicks" title="William Hicks">William Hicks</a> set out to destroy the Mahdi. The Egyptian soldiers were miserable <i>fallāḥīn</i> conscripts who had no interest in being in the Sudan, much less in fighting the Mahdi and morale was so poor that Hicks had to chain his men together to prevent them from deserting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto16_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-158">[158]</a></sup> On 3–5 November 1883, the <a href="/wiki/Ansar_(Sudan)" title="Ansar (Sudan)"><i>Ansar</i></a> (whom the British called "Dervishes"), as the Mahdi's followers were known, had destroyed an Egyptian Army of 8,000 under Colonel Hicks at El Obeid, with only about 250 Egyptians surviving and Hicks being one of the slain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto16_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-158">[158]</a></sup> At El Obeid, the <i>Ansar</i> captured from the Egyptians a huge number of Remington rifles and ammunition cases together with a large number of Krupp artillery guns and their shells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto15_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto15-149">[149]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_El_Obeid" title="Battle of El Obeid">Battle of El Obeid</a>, Egyptian morale, never high to begin with simply collapsed, and the black flag of the Mahdi soon started to fly over many a town in the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto16_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-158">[158]</a></sup> By the end of 1883 the Egyptians held only the ports on the Red Sea and a narrow belt of land around the Nile in northern Sudan; in both cases naval power was the key factor as gunboats in the Red Sea and the Nile provided a degree of firepower that the <i>Ansar</i> could not cope with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto17_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto17-159">[159]</a></sup> The only other place to hold out for a time was mostly Christian Equatoria under <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha" title="Emin Pasha">Emin Pasha</a>. Following the destruction of Hicks's army, the Liberal Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> decided that the Sudan was not worth the trouble it would take to hang onto, and as such, the Sudan was to be abandoned to the Mahdi. In December 1883, the British government ordered Egypt to abandon the Sudan, but that was difficult to carry out, as it involved the withdrawal of thousands of Egyptian soldiers, civilian employees, and their families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of 1884, Gordon had no interest in the Sudan and had just been hired to work as an officer with the newly established Congo Free State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto17_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto17-159">[159]</a></sup> Gordon – despite or rather because of his war hero status – disliked publicity and tried to avoid the press when he was in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto17_159-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto17-159">[159]</a></sup> While staying with his sister in Southampton, Gordon received an unexpected visitor, namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Thomas_Stead" title="William Thomas Stead">William Thomas Stead</a>, the editor of the <i>Pall Mall Gazette</i>, whom Gordon reluctantly agreed to do an interview with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Gordon wanted to talk about the Congo, but Stead kept on pressing him to talk about the Sudan; finally after much promoting on Stead's part Gordon opened up and attacked Gladstone's Sudan policy, coming out for an intervention to crush the Mahdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Gordon offered up a 19th-century version of the domino theory, claiming:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The danger arises from the influence which the spectacle of a conquering Mahometan [Muslim] Power established close to your frontiers will exercise upon the population which you govern. In all the cities of Egypt it will be felt that what the Mahdi has done they may do; and, as he has driven out the intruder, they may do the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto18_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto18-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Stead published his interview on 9 January 1884 on the front page of the <i>Pall Mall Gazette</i> alongside the leader (editorial) he had written entitled "Chinese Gordon for the Sudan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto18_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto18-163">[163]</a></sup> Urban wrote: "With this leader, William Stead's real motive in going to Southampton revealed itself at last. As to who tipped him off that the general would be staying here for just a couple of nights, we can only speculate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stead's interview caused a media sensation and led to a popular clamour for Gordon to be sent to the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto19_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto19-165">[165]</a></sup> Urban wrote: "The <i>Pall Mall Gazette</i> articles, in short, began a new chapter in international relations; powerful men using media manipulation of public opinion to trigger war. It is often suggested that that campaign by William Randolph Hearst's paper that led to the US invasion of Cuba in 1898 was the world's first episode of this kind, but the British press deserves these dubious laurels for its actions a full fourteen years earlier".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto19_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto19-165">[165]</a></sup> The man behind the campaign was the Adjutant General, Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garnet_Wolseley" title="Garnet Wolseley">Garnet Wolseley</a> – a skilled media manipulator who often leaked information to the press to effect changes in policy – and who was strongly opposed to Gladstone's policy of pulling out of the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> In 1880, the Liberals had won the general election on a platform of imperial retrenchment, and Gladstone had put his principles into practice by withdrawing from the Transvaal and Afghanistan in 1881. There was an imperialist "ultra" faction in the War Office led by Wolseley that felt that the Liberal government were too inclined to withdraw from various places all over the globe at the first sign of trouble, and who were determined to sabotage the withdrawal from the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Gordon and Wolseley were good friends (Wolseley was one of the people Gordon prayed for every night), and after a meeting with Wolseley at the War Office to discuss the crisis in the Sudan, Gordon left convinced that he had to go to the Sudan to "carry out the work of God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto16_158-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto16-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>With public opinion demanding that Gordon be sent to the Sudan, on 16 January 1884 the Gladstone government decided to send Gordon to the Sudan, albeit with the very limited mandate to report on the situation and advise on the best means of carrying out the evacuation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Gladstone had gone to his estate at Hawarden to recover from an illness and thus was not present at the meeting on 18 January where Gordon was given the Sudan command, but he was under the impression that Gordon's mission was advisory whereas the four ministers present at the meeting had given Gordon the impression that his mission was executive in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Gladstone felt that this was a deft political move; public opinion would be satisfied with "Chinese Gordon" going to the Sudan, but at the same time, Gordon was given such a limited mandate that the evacuation would proceed as planned. The Cabinet felt very uncomfortable with the appointment as they had been pressured by the press to send a man who was opposed to their Sudan policy to take command in the Sudan with the Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Granville_Leveson-Gower,_2nd_Earl_Granville" title="Granville Leveson-Gower, 2nd Earl Granville">Lord Grenville</a> wondering if they had just committed a "gigantic folly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Gordon made a short trip to Brussels to tell Leopold that he would not be going to the Congo after all, news that enraged the King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_C._G._Gordon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8346" data-file-width="6035" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/General_C._G._Gordon.jpg/220px-General_C._G._Gordon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/General_C._G._Gordon.jpg/330px-General_C._G._Gordon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/General_C._G._Gordon.jpg/440px-General_C._G._Gordon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_C._G._Gordon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General Gordon in Khartoum</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The British government asked Gordon to proceed to Khartoum to report on the best method of carrying out the evacuation. Gordon started for Cairo in January 1884, accompanied by Lt. Col. <a href="/wiki/John_Donald_Hamill_Stewart" title="John Donald Hamill Stewart">J. D. H. Stewart</a>. At Cairo, he received further instructions from Sir <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Baring,_1st_Earl_of_Cromer" title="Evelyn Baring, 1st Earl of Cromer">Evelyn Baring</a>, and was appointed governor-general with executive powers by the Khedive <a href="/wiki/Tewfik_Pasha" title="Tewfik Pasha">Tewfik Pasha</a>, who also gave Gordon a <i>firman</i> (edict) ordering him to establish a government in the Sudan, which Gordon was later to use as a reason for staying in Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup> Baring disapproved of sending Gordon to the Sudan, writing in a report to London that: "A man who habitually consults the Prophet Isaiah when he is in a difficulty is not apt to obey the orders of anyone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Gordon immediately confirmed Baring's fears as he started to issue press statements attacking the rebels as "a feeble lot of stinking Dervishes" and demanded he be allowed to "smash up the Mahdi".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto21_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto21-173">[173]</a></sup> Gordon sent a telegram to Khartoum reading: "Don't be panic-stricken. Ye are men, not women. I am coming. Gordon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>Urban wrote that Gordon's "most stupid mistake" occurred when he revealed his secret orders at a meeting of tribal leaders on 12 February at Berber, explaining that the Egyptians were pulling out, leading to almost all of the Arab tribes of northern Sudan declaring their loyalty to the Mahdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto21_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto21-173">[173]</a></sup> Given that Gordon himself in his interview with Stead had stated: "The moment it is known that we have given up the game, every man will go over to the Mahdi", his decision to reveal that the Egyptians were pulling out remains inexplicable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto21_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto21-173">[173]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, Gordon wrote what Urban called a "bizarre" letter to the Mahdi telling him to accept the authority of the Khedive of Egypt and offering him the chance to work as one of Gordon's provincial governors; the Mahdi contemptuously rejected Gordon's offer and sent back a letter demanding Gordon convert to Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto21_173-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto21-173">[173]</a></sup> The Mahdi ended his letter with the remark: "I am the Expected Mahdi and I do not boast! I am the successor of God's Prophet and I have no need of any sultanate of Kordofan or anywhere else!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto71_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto71-175">[175]</a></sup> Even Wolsely had cause to regret sending Gordon as Gordon revealed himself to be a "loose cannon" whose press statements attacking the Liberal government were "obstructing rather than furthering his plans to take over the Sudan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto21_173-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto21-173">[173]</a></sup> Traveling through Korosko and Berber, he arrived at Khartoum on 18 February, where he offered his earlier foe, the slaver-king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sebehr_Rahma" title="Sebehr Rahma">Rahama Zobeir</a>, release from prison in exchange for leading troops against Ahmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Gordon's abrupt mood swings and contradictory advice confirmed the Cabinet's view of him as mercurial and unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> Even an observer as sympathetic as <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> wrote about Gordon: "Mercury uncontrolled by the force of gravity was not on several occasions more unstable than Charles Gordon. His moods were capricious and uncertain, his passions violent, his impulses sudden and inconsistent. The mortal enemy of the morning had become a trusted ally by night".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto72_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto72-177">[177]</a></sup> The novelist <a href="/wiki/John_Buchan" title="John Buchan">John Buchan</a> wrote Gordon was so "unlike other men that he readily acquired a spiritual ascendency over all who knew him well and many who did not...", but at the same time Gordon had a "dualism", in that "the impression of single-heartedness was an illusion, for all his life his soul was the stage of conflict".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto72_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto72-177">[177]</a></sup> Gordon's attempt to have his former archenemy Zobeir, the "King of the Slavers" whom he had hunted for years and whose son he had executed installed as the new Sultan of the Sudan appalled Gladstone and offended his former admirers in the Anti-Slavery Society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taking_a_stand">Taking a stand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Taking a stand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After arriving in Khartoum, Gordon announced that on the grounds of honor, he would not evacuate Khartoum, but rather, would hold the city against the Mahdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup> Gordon was well received by a crowd of about 9,000 during his return to Khartoum where the crowd continually chanted, "Father!" and "Sultan!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto71_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto71-175">[175]</a></sup> Gordon assured the people of Khartoum in a speech delivered in his rough-hewn Arabic that the Mahdi was coming with his Army of Islam marching under their black banners, but to have no fear as here he would be stopped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto71_175-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto71-175">[175]</a></sup> Gordon had a garrison of about 8,000 soldiers armed with Remington rifles together with a colossal ammunition dump containing millions of rounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto22_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto22-178">[178]</a></sup> Gordon commenced the task of sending the women, children, the sick and wounded to Egypt. About 2,500 people had been removed before the Mahdi's forces closed in on Khartoum. Gordon hoped to have the influential local leader Sebehr Rahma appointed to take control of Sudan, but the British government refused to support a former slaver. During this time in Khartoum, Gordon befriended the Irish journalist Frank Powers, who was <i>The Times</i> (London) correspondent in the Sudan. Powers was delighted that the charismatic Gordon had no anti-Catholic prejudices and treated him as an equal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto23_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto23-179">[179]</a></sup> The hero-worshiping Powers wrote about Gordon: "He is indeed I believe the greatest man of this century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto23_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto23-179">[179]</a></sup> Gordon granted Powers privileged access and in return Powers started to write a series of popular articles for <i>The Times</i> depicting Gordon as the solitary hero taking on a vast horde of fanatical Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto23_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto23-179">[179]</a></sup> Gordon made all of his personal dispatches to London public (there was no Official Secrets Act at the time) in attempts to win public opinion over to his policy, writing on one dispatch: "Not secret as far as I am concerned".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto24_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto24-180">[180]</a></sup> At one point, Gordon suggested in a telegram to Gladstone that the notoriously corrupt Ottoman Sultan Abdul-Hamid II could be bribed into sending 3,000 Ottoman troops for the relief of Khartoum and if the British government was unwilling and/or unable to pay that amount, he was certain that either <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> or a group of American millionaires would be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>The advance of the rebels against Khartoum was combined with a revolt in the eastern Sudan. Colonel <a href="/wiki/Valentine_Baker" title="Valentine Baker">Valentine Baker</a> led an Egyptian force out of Suakin and was badly defeated by 1,000 Haddendowa warriors who declared their loyalty to the Mahdi under <a href="/wiki/Osman_Digna" title="Osman Digna">Osman Digna</a> at Al-Teb with 2,225 Egyptian soldiers and 96 officers killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto8_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto8-90">[90]</a></sup> Because the Egyptian troops at Suakin were repeatedly defeated, a British force was sent to Suakin under General Sir <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Graham" title="Gerald Graham">Gerald Graham</a>, which drove the rebels away in several hard-fought actions. At Tamai on 13 March 1884, Graham was attacked by the Haddendowa (whom the British disparagingly called "Fuzzy Wuzzies") whom he defeated, but in the course of the battle, the Haddendowa broke the <a href="/wiki/Black_Watch" title="Black Watch">Black Watch</a> square, an action later celebrated in the Kipling poem "<a href="/wiki/Fuzzy-Wuzzy" title="Fuzzy-Wuzzy">Fuzzy-Wuzzy</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> The ferocity of the Haddendowa attacks astonished the British, and Graham argued that he needed more troops if he were to advance deeper into the Sudan while one newspaper correspondent reported that the average British soldiers did not understand why they were in the Sudan killing "such brave fellows" for "the sake of the wretched Egyptians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto24_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto24-180">[180]</a></sup> Gordon urged that the road from Suakin to Berber be opened, but his request was refused by the government in London, and in April Graham and his forces were withdrawn and Gordon and the Sudan were abandoned. The garrison at Berber surrendered in May, and Khartoum was completely isolated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon decided to stay and hold Khartoum despite the orders of the Gladstone government to merely report about the best means of supervising the evacuation of the Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup> Powers who acted as Gordon's unofficial press attaché wrote in <i>The Times</i>: "We are daily expecting British troops. We cannot bring ourselves to believe that we are to be abandoned".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto25_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto25-184">[184]</a></sup> In his diary, Gordon wrote: "I own to having been very insubordinate to Her Majesty's Government and its officials, but it is my nature, and I cannot help it. I fear I have not even tried to play battledore and shuttlecock with them. I know if <i>I</i> was chief I would never employ <i>myself</i> for I am incorrigible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup> Because of public opinion, the government dared not sack Gordon, but the Cabinet was extremely angry about Gordon's insubordination with many privately saying Gordon if wanted to defy orders by holding Khartoum, then he only deserved what he was going to get.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto25_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto25-184">[184]</a></sup> Gladstone himself took Gordon's attacks on his Sudan policy very personally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto25_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto25-184">[184]</a></sup> One Cabinet minister wrote: "The London newspapers and the Tories clamor for an expedition to Khartoum, the former from ignorance, the latter because it is the best model of embarrassing us...Of course it is not an impossible undertaking, but it is melancholy to think of the waste of lives and the treasure which it must involve".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto25_184-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto25-184">[184]</a></sup> The Cabinet itself was divided and confused about just what to do about the Sudan crisis, leading to a highly dysfunctional style of decision-making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-7"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 220px;">
<div style="width: 220px; height: 152px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -2px; left: -3px; width: 228px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_(1884).jpg"><img alt="10 piastre promissory note issued and hand-signed by Gen. Gordon during the Siege of Khartoum (26 April 1884)[185]" data-file-height="4648" data-file-width="3436" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_%281884%29.jpg/228px-SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_%281884%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_%281884%29.jpg/342px-SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_%281884%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_%281884%29.jpg/456px-SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_%281884%29.jpg 2x" width="228"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SUD-S103a-Siege_of_Khartoum-10_Piastres_(1884).jpg" title="File:SUD-S103a-Siege of Khartoum-10 Piastres (1884).jpg">File:SUD-S103a-Siege of Khartoum-10 Piastres (1884).jpg</a></div>
<center>10 <a href="/wiki/Piastre" title="Piastre">piastre</a> promissory note issued and hand-signed by Gen. Gordon during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Khartoum" title="Siege of Khartoum">Siege of Khartoum</a> (26 April 1884)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gordon had a strong death wish, and clearly wanted to die fighting at Khartoum, writing in a letter to his sister: "I feel so very much inclined to wish it His will might be my release. Earth's joys grow very dim, its glories have faded".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup> In his biography of Gordon, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Nutting" title="Anthony Nutting">Anthony Nutting</a> wrote Gordon was obsessed with "the ever-present, constantly repeated desire for martyrdom and for that glorious immortality in union with God and away from the wretchedness of life on this earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto20_171-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto20-171">[171]</a></sup> Because his Turkish, Egyptian and many of his Sudanese troops were Muslim, Gordon refrained in public from describing his battle with the Mahdi as a religious war, but Gordon's diary showed he viewed himself as a Christian champion fighting against the Mahdi just as much for God as for Queen and country. The Mahdi and his followers had been fighting a <i>jihad</i> since 1881 and looked forward to taking on the famous General Gordon as a chance to win glory for Allah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon energetically organized the defence of Khartoum right from the moment he arrived in Khartoum, using his training as a military engineer to turn the city into a fortress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto22_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto22-178">[178]</a></sup> Additionally, Gordon had guns and armored plating attached to the paddle wheel streamers stationed at Khartoum to create his own private riverine navy that served as an effective force against the <i>Ansar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto26_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto26-187">[187]</a></sup> The Turkish troops at Khartoum were not part of the Ottoman Army, but rather <i>bashi-bazouks</i>, irregulars whom Gordon commented were good for raids, but useless for battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto26_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto26-187">[187]</a></sup> The Shaggyeh (one of the few Arab tribes who did not rally to the Mahdi) drove Gordon to distraction with Gordon writing in his diary about them: "Dreadful lot! How I look forward to their disbandment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto27_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto27-188">[188]</a></sup> Gordon had a low opinion of the Egyptian, Turkish and Arab Sudanese troops under his command-whom he constantly disparaged as a mutinous, badly disciplined and ill-trained rabble good only for looting – but had a much higher opinion of his black Sudanese soldiers, most of them former slaves who would rather die fighting as free men than live as slaves again; it was well known that the Mahdi's forces were going to enslave the blacks of Khartoum once they took the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto27_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto27-188">[188]</a></sup> The black Sudanese troops, many from what is now South Sudan, proved to be Gordon's best troops at Khartoum and numbered about twenty-three hundred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto8_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto8-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_siege_begins">The siege begins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: The siege begins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A siege by the Mahdist forces commanded by the Mahdi himself started on 18 March 1884. Initially, the siege of Khartoum was more in nature a blockade rather than a true siege as the Mahdi's forces lacked the strength to wage a proper siege, for example only cutting the telegraphy lines in April 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto25_184-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto25-184">[184]</a></sup> The British had decided to abandon the Sudan, but it was clear that Gordon had other plans, and the public increasingly called for a relief expedition. Gordon's last telegrams were clearly meant for the British public with one message addressed to Baring reading: "You state your intention of not sending any relief force up here to Berber...I shall hold on here as long as I can, and if I can suppress the rebellion, I shall do so. If I cannot, I shall retire to the Equator and leave you with the indelible disgrace of abandoning the garrisons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Gladstone was opposed to hanging onto the Sudan, saying in a speech in the House of Commons that sending a relief force to Khartoum would be "a war of conquest against a people struggling to be free. Yes, these are people struggling to be free and rightly struggling to be free".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Khartoum was surrounded by the <i>Ansar</i> in March 1884, but was not cut off from the outside world for a considerable time afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup> Gordon's armoured steamers continued to sail in and out of Khartoum with little difficulty for the first six months of the siege, and it was not until September 1884 that the armoured steamers first had trouble reaching the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup> Gordon himself had a low opinion of his enemy, writing that the <i>Ansar</i> besieging him were "some 500 determined men and some 2, 000 rag-tag Arabs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup> Nutting wrote that Gordon "could have withdrawn at almost any moment between March and May" if only he had been willing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup> The American historian James Perry wrote: "But instead of following instructions, he stayed put, longing for martyrdom. It wasn't exactly fair to the Egyptian garrisons he had been sent to evacuate; they had no death wish".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup> On 25 July 1884, the Cabinet over the objections of the Prime Minister voted to send a relief expedition to Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup> On 5 August 1884, the House of Commons voted to send the relief force with a budget of £300, 000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto28_191-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto28-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this time, Gordon, when he was not organising the besieged garrison with incredible energy, spent his time writing a somewhat rambling diary containing his reflections on the siege, life, fate, and his own intense, idiosyncratic version of Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto31_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto31-192">[192]</a></sup> Gordon waged a very vigorous defense, sending out his armoured steamers to shoot up the <i>Ansar</i> camps along the Blue Nile while he regularly made raids on the besiegers that often gave the Madhi's forces a bloody nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto31_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto31-192">[192]</a></sup> Elated by these successes, Gordon wrote in his diary: "We are going to hold out here forever".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto31_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto31-192">[192]</a></sup> To keep up morale, Gordon had a military band perform concerts in the central plaza every Friday and Sunday evenings for free, and cast his own decorations for his men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry.2C_2005_p._177_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perry.2C_2005_p._177-193">[193]</a></sup> Though the telegraph lines to Cairo were cut, Gordon used the remaining telegraph lines to build his own telegraph network within Khartoum linking the men holding the walls of Khartoum to Governor-General's palace, thus keeping him well informed of what was happening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry.2C_2005_p._177_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perry.2C_2005_p._177-193">[193]</a></sup> To slow down the <i>Ansar</i> assaults, Gordon built primitive landmines out of water cans stuffed with dynamite and to confuse the enemy about his numbers, he put up wooden dummies in uniform along the walls of Khartoum facing the Blue Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry.2C_2005_p._177_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-Perry.2C_2005_p._177-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was not until August 1884 that the government decided to take steps to relieve Gordon, with the British relief force, called the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Expedition" title="Nile Expedition">Nile Expedition</a>, or, more popularly, the Khartoum Relief Expedition or Gordon Relief Expedition (a title that Gordon strongly deprecated). The relief force, under the command of Gordon's old friend Field Marshal Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garnet_Wolseley" title="Garnet Wolseley">Garnet Wolseley</a>, was not ready until November 1884.
