Wolseley had earlier served in Canada where he had commanded the Red River expedition of 1870, during which time he gained considerable respect for the skills of French-Canadian <i><a href="/wiki/Voyageurs" title="Voyageurs">voyageurs</a></i>, and now insisted he could not travel up the Nile without the <i>voyageurs</i> to assist his men as river pilots and boatmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto30_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto30-194">[194]</a></sup> It took considerable time to hire the <i>voyageurs</i> in Canada and bring them to Egypt, which delayed the expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto30_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto30-194">[194]</a></sup> Some of the "<i>voyageurs</i>" who arrived in Egypt turned out to be lawyers led by an alderman from Toronto who wanted to see "the fun" of war and were useless as boatmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto30_194-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto30-194">[194]</a></sup> Wolseley was a bureaucratic general whose talents lay in administrative work, and as a field commander, Wolseley was slow, methodical, and cautious, making him in the opinion of Urban supremely unqualified to lead the relief expedition as he found one excuse after an another to proceed down the Nile at a sluggish pace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> For example, Wolseley could have hired Egyptian boatmen who knew the Nile to serve as river pilots instead of bringing over <i>voyageurs</i> from Canada, who knew nothing of the Nile, and moreover Wolseley only called for the <i>voyageurs</i> after his arrival in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto30_194-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto30-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_brink">On the brink</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: On the brink">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 4 September 1884, Gordon's fortunes took a turn for the worse when the most able of his subordinates Mohammed Aly together with about 1, 000 of Gordon's best troops were killed in an ambush, while conducting a raid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto31_192-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto31-192">[192]</a></sup> Gordon wrote in his diary that Mohammed Aly had captured "a lad of 12 or 14 years of age, and the little chap spoke out boldly, and said he believed Mohamed Ahmed was the Mahdi and that we were dogs. He was shot! Before I heard of our defeat I heard of this, and I thought, 'THAT will not pass unavenged'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto31_192-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto31-192">[192]</a></sup> On 9 September 1884, an armored streamer, the <i>Abbas</i> on its way to Cairo was captured by the <i>Ansar</i> for the first time and all aboard were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> Amongst the dead were Gordon's unofficial spokesman, the passionate wordsmith and <i>Times</i> journalist Frank Powers, Gordon's Chief of Staff Colonel <a href="/wiki/John_Donald_Hamill_Stewart" title="John Donald Hamill Stewart">Stewart</a> and the French consul in Khartoum Herbin, all of whom Gordon was sending to Cairo to plead for relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Gordon received a letter from the Mahdi taunting him over the murders of his friends Powers and Stewart, warning that he would be next if he did not surrender. Bitterly Gordon wrote in his diary: "It is impossible to have any more words with Mohammed Achmed, only lead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto73_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto73-197">[197]</a></sup> Amongst the papers captured on the <i>Abbas</i> was the cipher key Gordon used to code his messages in and out of Khartoum, which meant he could no longer read the messages he received, leading him to write in his diary: "I think cipher-messages are in some countries, like this, a mistake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto31_192-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto31-192">[192]</a></sup> During this period, Gordon was lionized by the British press, which portrayed him as a latter-day Christian "knight", a "crusader" and a "saint", a man of pure good, heroically battling the Mahdi, who was depicted as a man of pure evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto33_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto33-198">[198]</a></sup> The <i>Pall Mall Gazette</i> in a front page leader wrote that Gordon stood "out in clear relief against the Eastern sky. Alone in a black continent, dauntless and unfaltering, he discharges his great trust, holding the capital of the Sudan against the beleaguering hordes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> The defenses Gordon had built with lines of earthwork, mines, and barbed wire presented the <i>Ansar</i> with much difficulty and their attempts to storm Khartoum failed, but the <i>Ansar</i> made good use of their Krupp artillery to gradually batter down the defenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> To counter Gordon's armored streamers, the Mahdi built a series of forts along the Nile equipped with Krupp guns that over time proceeded to make it almost impossible for Gordon's navy to operate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22At_Last!%22_(1885)_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="670" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/%22At_Last%21%22_%281885%29_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg/220px-%22At_Last%21%22_%281885%29_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/%22At_Last%21%22_%281885%29_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg/330px-%22At_Last%21%22_%281885%29_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/%22At_Last%21%22_%281885%29_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg/440px-%22At_Last%21%22_%281885%29_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22At_Last!%22_(1885)_Major-General_Charles_George_Gordon_greeting_reinforcements_at_Khartoum_in_1885-_TIMEA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cartoon" title="Cartoon">Cartoon</a> of Charles Gordon greeting reinforcements at Khartoum in 1885. Published before Gordon's death was known.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the end of 1884, both the garrison and the population of Khartoum were starving to death; there were no horses, donkeys, cats, or dogs left in Khartoum as the people had eaten all of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto34_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto34-200">[200]</a></sup> Gordon told the civilians of Khartoum that anyone who wished to leave, even to join the Mahdi's army were free to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto34_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto34-200">[200]</a></sup> About half of the population took up his offer to promptly leave the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto34_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto34-200">[200]</a></sup> A note written by Gordon and dated December 14 was sent out by a messenger from Khartoum who reached Wolseley's army on 30 December 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto35_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto35-201">[201]</a></sup> The note read "Khartoum all right. Can hold out for years. C.G. Gordon", but the messenger (who knew very little English) had memorised another, darker message from Gordon, namely: "We want you to come quickly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-8"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto35_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto35-201">[201]</a></sup> In the same month, Gordon received a letter from the Mahdi offering safe passage out of Khartoum: "We have written to you to go back to your country...I repeat to you the words of Allah, Do not destroy yourself. Allah Himself is merciful to you".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto34_200-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto34-200">[200]</a></sup> Gordon and the Mahdi never met, but the two men, both charismatic and intensely religiously soldiers who saw themselves as fighting for God had developed a grudging mutual respect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto34_200-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto34-200">[200]</a></sup> However, Faught wrote that there was a fundamental difference between Gordon and the Mahdi In that Gordon never tried to convert the Muslims of the Sudan to Christianity whereas the Mahdi was an "Islamic extremist" who believed he would establish a world-wide caliphate, looking forward to the day when he would "see the world bow before him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto74_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto74-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>During November–December 1884, Gordon's diary showed the stressful effects of the siege, as he was in a state of mental exhaustion, a man on the brink of madness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> In his final months Gordon oscillated between a longing for martyrdom and death vs. an intense horror at the prospect of his own demise as the hour of his destruction rapidly approached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-6"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> Even if the relief force had reached him, it is not clear if he would had left Khartoum as Gordon wrote in his diary: "If any emissary or letter comes up here ordering me to come down I WILL NOT OBEY IT, BUT WILL STAY HERE, AND FALL WITH THE TOWN!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-7"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> At an another point, a death-obsessed Gordon wrote in his diary: "Better a bullet to the brain than to flicker out unheeded".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-8"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> In a letter that reached Cairo in December, Gordon wrote: "Farewell. You will never hear from me again. I fear that there will be treachery in the garrison, and all will be over by Christmas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about1_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-about1-203">[203]</a></sup> On 14 December 1884, Gordon wrote the last entry in his diary, which read: "Now MARK THIS, if the Expeditionary Force and I ask for no more than two hundred men, does not come in ten days, <i>the town may fall</i>; and I have done my best for the honour of our country. Goodbye, C. G. Gordon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perry.2C_2005_p._193_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perry.2C_2005_p._193-204">[204]</a></sup> A chain-smoking Gordon constantly paced the roof of his palace during the day, looking vainly for smoke on the Nile, indicating that the steamers were coming while spending much of the rest of his time in prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto34_200-5"><a href="#cite_note-auto34-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 January 1885, the <i>Ansar</i> took the fort at Omdurman, which allowed them to use their Krupp artillery to bring down enfilading fire on the defenses of Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-9"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> In one of the last letters Gordon had smuggled out, he wrote: "I expect Her Majesty's Government are in a precious rage with me for holding out, and so forcing their hands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-10"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> In his last weeks, those who knew Gordon described him as a chain-smoking, rage-filled, desperate man wearing a shabby uniform who spent hours talking to a mouse that he shared his office with when he was not attacking his Sudanese servants with his rattan cane during one of his rages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> A particular aspect of Gordon's personality that stood out was his death wish as everyone who knew him was convinced that he wanted to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-11"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> When a Lebanese merchant visited Gordon in the evening, the <i>Ansar</i> began an artillery bombardment, leading the frightened merchant to suggest that perhaps Gordon ought to dim the lights to avoid drawing enemy fire down on the palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto32_186-12"><a href="#cite_note-auto32-186">[186]</a></sup> The merchant recalled Gordon's response: "He called up the guard and gave the orders to shoot me if I moved" and ordered all of the lamps in the palace to be lit up as brightly as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto36_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto36-206">[206]</a></sup> Gordon defiantly told the merchant: "Go tell all the people of Khartoum that Gordon fears nothing, for God has created him without fear!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto36_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto36-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_end">The end</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The end">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The relief force consisted of two groups, a "flying column" of camel-borne troops from <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Halfa" title="Wadi Halfa">Wadi Halfa</a>. The troops reached Korti towards the end of December, and arrived at Metemma on 20 January 1885. There they found four gunboats which had been sent north by Gordon four months earlier, and prepared them for the trip back up the Nile. On 18 January 1885, the advance guard of Wolseley's force under <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Stewart" title="Herbert Stewart">Herbert Stewart</a> defeated the <i>Ansar</i> force sent to stop the relief force at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Abu_Klea" title="Battle of Abu Klea">Battle of Abu Klea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto73_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto73-197">[197]</a></sup> When the news of the defeat reached <i>Ansar</i> besieging Khartoum, terrible cries of lamentation aroused from the besieging force, which led Gordon to guess that the <i>Ansar</i> had been defeated in battle and that Wolseley must be close.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto73_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto73-197">[197]</a></sup> On 24 January two of the steamers, carrying 20 soldiers of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Sussex_Regiment" title="Royal Sussex Regiment">Sussex Regiment</a> wearing red tunics to clearly identify them as British, were sent on a reconnaissance mission to Khartoum, with orders from Wolseley not to attempt to rescue Gordon or bring him ammunition or food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-207">[207]</a></sup> On the evening of 24 January 1885, the Mahdi met with his generals whose leading spokesman was his uncle Muhammad Abd al-Karim, who told him that, with the Nile low and Wolseley close, it was time to either storm Khartoum or retreat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto37_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto37-208">[208]</a></sup> On the morning of 26 January 1885, the <i>Ansar</i> began their final attack by storming the city via the gap in the defense caused by the low Nile and after an hour's fighting, the starving defenders had abandoned the fight and the city was theirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto37_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto37-208">[208]</a></sup> The <i>Ansar</i> took no prisoners and all of the approximately 7, 000 defenders were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about1_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-about1-203">[203]</a></sup> On arriving at Khartoum on 28 January, the relief force found that the city had been captured and Gordon had been killed just two days before, coincidentally, two days before his 52nd birthday. Under heavy fire from <i>Ansar</i> warriors on the bank, the two steamers turned back up river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>The British press criticized the relief force for arriving two days late, but it was later argued that the Mahdi's forces had good intelligence, and if the camel corps had advanced earlier, the final attack on Khartoum would also have come earlier. Finally, the boats sent were not there to relieve Gordon, who was not expected to agree to abandon the city, and the small force and limited supplies on board could have offered scant military support for the besieged in any case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="586" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg/220px-General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg/330px-General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg/440px-General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_Gordon%27s_Last_Stand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>General Gordon's Last Stand</i>, by <a href="/wiki/George_W._Joy" title="George W. Joy">George W. Joy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The manner of his death is uncertain, but it was romanticized in a popular painting by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_William_Joy" title="George William Joy">George William Joy</a> – <i>General Gordon's Last Stand</i> (1893, currently in the Leeds City Art Gallery), and again in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Khartoum_(film)" title="Khartoum (film)">Khartoum</a></i> (1966) with <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> as Gordon. The most popular account of Gordon's death was that he put on his ceremonial gold-braided blue uniform of the Governor-General together with the Pasha's red fez and that he went out unarmed except with his rattan cane to be cut down by the <i>Ansar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-9"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> This account was very popular with the British public as it contained much Christian imagery with Gordon as a Christ-like figure dying passively for the sins of all humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-10"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon was apparently killed at the Governor-General's palace about an hour before dawn. As recounted in Bernard M. Allen’s article "How Khartoum Fell" (1941), the Mahdi had given strict orders to his three Khalifas not to kill Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> However, the orders were not obeyed. Gordon's Sudanese servants later stated that Gordon for once did not go out armed only with his rattan cane, but also took with him a loaded revolver and his sword, and died in mortal combat fighting the <i>Ansar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Gordon died on the steps of a stairway in the northwestern corner of the palace, where he and his personal bodyguard, Agha Khalil Orphali, had been firing at the enemy. Orphali was knocked unconscious and did not see Gordon die. When he woke up again that afternoon, he found Gordon's body covered with flies and the head cut off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> A merchant, Bordeini Bey, glimpsed Gordon standing on the palace steps in a white uniform looking into the darkness. The best evidence suggests that Gordon went out to confront the enemy, gunned down several of the <i>Ansar</i> with his revolver and after running out of bullets, drew his sword only to be shot down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-11"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> Reference is made to an 1889 account of the General surrendering his sword to a senior Mahdist officer, then being struck and subsequently speared in the side as he rolled down the staircase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carl_von_Slatin" title="Rudolf Carl von Slatin">Rudolf Slatin</a>, the Austrian governor of Darfur who had been taken prisoner by the <i>Ansar</i> wrote that three soldiers showed him Gordon's head at his tent before delivering it to the Mahdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> When Gordon's head was unwrapped at the Mahdi's feet, he ordered the head transfixed between the branches of a tree ". . . where all who passed it could look in disdain, children could throw stones at it and the hawks of the desert could sweep and circle above."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pakenham.2C_T_1912.2C_p._272_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pakenham.2C_T_1912.2C_p._272-215">[215]</a></sup> His body was desecrated and thrown down a well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pakenham.2C_T_1912.2C_p._272_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pakenham.2C_T_1912.2C_p._272-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SLATIN(1896)_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1269" data-file-width="1689" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/SLATIN%281896%29_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg/220px-SLATIN%281896%29_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/SLATIN%281896%29_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg/330px-SLATIN%281896%29_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/SLATIN%281896%29_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg/440px-SLATIN%281896%29_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SLATIN(1896)_p385_BRINGING_GORDON%27S_HEAD_TO_SLATIN.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gordon's head shown to Slatin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the hours following Gordon's death an estimated 10,000 civilians and members of the garrison were killed in Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pakenham.2C_T_1912.2C_p._272_215-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pakenham.2C_T_1912.2C_p._272-215">[215]</a></sup> The massacre was finally halted by orders of the Mahdi. Many of Gordon's papers were saved and collected by two of his sisters, Helen Clark Gordon, who married Gordon's medical colleague in China, Dr. Moffit, and Mary, who married Gerald Henry Blunt. Gordon's papers, as well as some of his grandfather's (Samuel Enderby III), were accepted by the British Library around 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>The failure to rescue General Gordon's force in Sudan was a major blow to Prime Minister Gladstone's popularity. Queen Victoria sent him a telegram of rebuke which found its way into the press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto38_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto38-217">[217]</a></sup> Victoria's telegram was not coded as usual which suggests she wanted it to appear in the press. Critics said Gladstone had neglected military affairs and had not acted promptly enough to save the besieged Gordon. Critics inverted his acronym, "G.O.M." (for "Grand Old Man"), to "M.O.G." (for "Murderer of Gordon"). Gladstone told the Cabinet that the public cared much about Gordon and nothing about the Sudan, so he ordered Wolseley home after learning of Gordon's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto39_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto39-218">[218]</a></sup> Wolseley, who had been led to believe that his expedition was the initial phase of the British conquest of the Sudan was furious, and in a telegram to Queen Victoria contemptuously called Gladstone "...the tradesman who has become a politician".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto39_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto39-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1885, Gordon achieved the martyrdom he been seeking at Khartoum as the British press portrayed him as a saintly Christian hero and martyr who had died nobly resisting the Islamic onslaught of the Mahdi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> As late as 1901 on the anniversary of Gordon's death, <i>The Times</i> wrote in a leader (editorial) that Gordon was "that solitary figure holding aloft the flag of England in the face of the dark hordes of Islam".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto33_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto33-198">[198]</a></sup> Gordon's death caused a huge wave of national grief all over Britain with 13 March 1885 being set aside as a day of mourning for the "fallen hero of Khartoum".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto38_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto38-217">[217]</a></sup> In a sermon, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Chichester" title="Bishop of Chichester">Bishop of Chichester</a> stated: "Nations who envied our greatness rejoiced now at our weakness and our inability to protect our trusted servant. Scorn and reproach were cast upon us, and would we plead that it was undeserved? No; the conscience of the nation felt that a strain rested upon it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto38_217-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto38-217">[217]</a></sup> Baring – who deeply disliked Gordon – wrote that because of the "national hysteria" caused by Gordon's death, that saying critical about him at present would be equal to questioning Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto38_217-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto38-217">[217]</a></sup> Stones were thrown at the windows at <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a> as Gladstone was denounced as the "Murderer of Gordon", the <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas</a> figure who betrayed the Christ figure Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-12"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> The wave of mourning was not just confined to Britain. In New York, Paris and Berlin, pictures of Gordon appeared in shop windows with black lining as all over the West the fallen general was seen as a Christ-like man who sacrificed himself resisting the advance of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-13"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the popular demand to "avenge Gordon", the Conservative government that came into office after the 1885 election did nothing of the sort as the Sudan was judged to be not worth the huge financial costs it would have taken to conquer it, the same conclusion that the Liberals had reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-14"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> After Khartoum, the Mahdi established his Islamic state which restored slavery and imposed a very harsh rule that according to one estimate caused the deaths of 8 million people between 1885–1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> In 1887, the <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha_Relief_Expedition" title="Emin Pasha Relief Expedition">Emin Pasha Relief Expedition</a> under <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> sent out to rescue Dr. <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha" title="Emin Pasha">Emin Pasha</a>, still holding out in Equatoria against the <i>Ansar</i>. Many have seen the attempt to save Emin Pasha, a German doctor-biologist-botanist who had converted from Judaism first to Lutheranism and then to Islam who been not particularly famous in Europe until then as a consolation prize for Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> Egypt had been in the French sphere of influence until 1882 when the British had occupied it. In March 1896 a French force under the command of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Marchand" title="Jean-Baptiste Marchand">Jean-Baptiste Marchand</a> left Dakar with the intention of marching across the Sahara with the aim of destroying the <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_Sudan" title="Mahdist Sudan"><i>Mahdiyah</i> state</a>. The French hoped that conquering the Sudan would allow them to lever the British out of Egypt, and thus restore Egypt to the French sphere of influence. To block the French, a British force under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener">Herbert Kitchener</a> was sent to destroy the <i>Mahdiyah</i> state and annihilated the <i>Ansar</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Omdurman" title="Battle of Omdurman">Battle of Omdurman</a> in 1898. It was thus imperial rivalry with the French, not a desire to "avenge Gordon" that led the British to end the <i>Mahdiyah</i> state in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-judd_11-15"><a href="#cite_note-judd-11">[11]</a></sup> However the British public and Kitchener himself saw the expedition as one to "avenge Gordon". As the Mahdi was long dead, Kitchener had to content himself with blowing up the Mahdi's tomb as revenge for Gordon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Omdurman" title="Battle of Omdurman">Battle of Omdurman</a>, Kitchener opened a letter from the Prime Minister, Lord Salisbury and for the first time learned the real purpose of the expedition to keep the French out of the Sudan and that "avenging Gordon" was merely a pretext.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg/220px-General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg/330px-General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg/440px-General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_Charles_George_Gordon_Statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
General Charles George Gordon Statue in <a href="/wiki/Gravesend" title="Gravesend">Gravesend</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2296" data-file-width="1688" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg/220px-Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg/330px-Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg/440px-Charles_George_Gordon_Statue_Melbourne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

Statue in Gordon Reserve, <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_Gordon_statue,_Embankment,_London.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="427" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/General_Gordon_statue%2C_Embankment%2C_London.jpg/220px-General_Gordon_statue%2C_Embankment%2C_London.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/General_Gordon_statue%2C_Embankment%2C_London.jpg/330px-General_Gordon_statue%2C_Embankment%2C_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/General_Gordon_statue%2C_Embankment%2C_London.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_Gordon_statue,_Embankment,_London.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue on the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Embankment" title="Victoria Embankment">Victoria Embankment</a>, London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>News of Gordon's death led to an "unprecedented wave of public grief across Britain." A memorial service, conducted by the Bishop of Newcastle, was held at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St. Paul's Cathedral">St. Paul's Cathedral</a> on 14 March. The <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_London" title="Lord Mayor of London">Lord Mayor of London</a> opened a public subscription to raise funds for a permanent memorial to Gordon; this eventually materialized as the Gordon Boys Home, now <a href="/wiki/Gordon%27s_School" title="Gordon's School">Gordon's School</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_End,_Woking" title="West End, Woking">West End, Woking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor83_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor83-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<p>Statues were erected in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>, London, in Chatham, Gravesend, Melbourne (Australia), and Khartoum. <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>, where Gordon had stayed with his sister, Augusta, in Rockstone Place before his departure to the Sudan, erected a memorial in Porter's Mead, now Queen's Park, near the town's docks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor83_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor83-224">[224]</a></sup> On 16 October 1885, the "light and elegant structure" was unveiled; it comprises a stone base on which there are four polished red Aberdeen granite columns, about twenty feet high. The columns are surmounted by carved capitals supporting a cross. The pedestal bears the arms of the Gordon clan and of the borough of Southampton, and also Gordon’s name in Chinese. Around the base is an inscription referring to Gordon as a soldier, philanthropist and administrator and mentions those parts of the world in which he served, closing with a quotation from his last letter to his sisters: "I am quite happy, thank God! and, like Lawrence, I have tried to do my duty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant146_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grant146-226">[226]</a></sup> The memorial is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grade_II_listed_building" title="Grade II listed building">Grade II listed building</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon's memory, as well as his work in supervising the town's riverside fortifications, is commemorated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravesend,_Kent" title="Gravesend, Kent">Gravesend</a>; the embankment of the Riverside Leisure Area is known as the Gordon Promenade, while Khartoum Place lies just to the south. Located in the town centre of his birthplace of Woolwich is General Gordon Square, formerly known as General Gordon Place until a major urban landscaped area was developed and the road name changed. In addition, one of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woolwich_Free_Ferry" title="Woolwich Free Ferry">Woolwich Free Ferry</a> vessels was named <i>Gordon</i> in his memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rogers_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1888 a statue of Gordon by <a href="/wiki/Hamo_Thornycroft" title="Hamo Thornycroft">Hamo Thornycroft</a> was erected in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, exactly halfway between the two fountains. It was removed in 1943. In a House of Commons speech on 5 May 1948, then opposition leader <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> spoke out in favour of the statue's return to its original location: "Is the right honorable Gentleman [the Minister of Works] aware that General Gordon was not only a military commander, who gave his life for his country, but, in addition, was considered very widely throughout this country as a model of a Christian hero, and that very many cherished ideals are associated with his name? Would not the right honorable Gentleman consider whether this statue [...] might not receive special consideration [...]? General Gordon was a figure outside and above the ranks of military and naval commanders." However, in 1953 the statue minus a large slice of its pedestal was reinstalled on the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Embankment" title="Victoria Embankment">Victoria Embankment</a>, in front of the newly built Ministry of Defence main buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>An identical statue by Thornycroft—but with the pedestal intact—is located in a small park called Gordon Reserve, near <a href="/wiki/Parliament_House,_Melbourne" title="Parliament House, Melbourne">Parliament House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corps_of_Royal_Engineers" title="Corps of Royal Engineers">Corps of Royal Engineers</a>, Gordon's own Corps, commissioned a statue of Gordon on a camel. It was exhibited at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> in 1890 and then erected in Brompton Barracks, Chatham, the home of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_School_of_Military_Engineering" title="Royal School of Military Engineering">Royal School of Military Engineering</a>, where it still stands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> Much later a second casting was made. In 1902 it was placed at the junction of St Martin's Lane and Charing Cross Road in London. In 1904 it was moved to Khartoum, where it stood at the intersection of Gordon Avenue and Victoria Avenue, 200 meters south of the new palace that had been built in 1899. It was removed in 1958, shortly after the Sudan became independent. This is the figure which, since April 1959, stands at the <a href="/wiki/Gordon%27s_School" title="Gordon's School">Gordon's School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Woking" title="Woking">Woking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon's Tomb, which was carved by <a href="/wiki/F._W._Pomeroy" title="F. W. Pomeroy">Frederick William Pomeroy</a>, lies in <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a>, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_Missionary_Society" title="Church Missionary Society">Church Missionary Society</a> (CMS) work in Sudan was undertaken under the name of the Gordon Memorial Mission. This was a very <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a> branch of CMS and was able to start work in Sudan in 1900 as soon as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Condominium" title="Anglo-Egyptian Condominium">Anglo-Egyptian Condominium</a> took control after the fall of Khartoum in 1899. In 1885 at a meeting in London, £3,000 were allocated to a Gordon Memorial Mission in Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Presidential Palace in Khartoum (built in 1899), in the west wing on the ground floor, there is (or once was) a stone slab against the wall on the left side of the main corridor when coming from the main entrance with the text: "Charles George Gordon died—26 Jan 1885," on the spot where Gordon was killed, at the foot of the stairs in the old Governor-General's Palace (built around 1850).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_portrayals_and_legacy">Media portrayals and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Media portrayals and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> played Gordon in the 1966 epic film <i><a href="/wiki/Khartoum_(film)" title="Khartoum (film)">Khartoum</a></i>, which deals with the siege of Khartoum. <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a> played Muhammad Ahmad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The British historian <a href="/wiki/Alex_von_Tunzelmann" title="Alex von Tunzelmann">Alex von Tunzelmann</a> criticized the film for portraying Gordon and the Mahdi regularly meeting and as <i>fréres ennemis</i>, through she added it is true Gordon and the Mahdi did exchange letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the six months after the British public learned of Gordon's death, newspapers and journals published hundreds of articles celebrating Gordon as a "saint".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto33_198-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto33-198">[198]</a></sup> The American historian Cynthia Behrman wrote the articles all commented upon "...Gordon's religious faith, his skill with native peoples, his fearlessness in the face of danger (a recurrent motif is Gordon's habit of leading his troops into battle armed with no more than a rattan cane), his honor, his resourcefulness, his graciousness to subordinates, his impatience with cant and hypocrisy, his hatred of glory and honors, his dislike of lionization and social rewards, and on and on. One begins to wonder whether the man had any faults at all".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto33_198-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto33-198">[198]</a></sup> "The reading public wanted heroes, it wanted to read about one lone Englishmen sacrificing himself for glory, honour, God, and the Empire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>Such was the popularity of Gordon that the first critical book by a British author was not published until 1908, when Baring – by this time raised to the peerage as Viscount Cromer – published his autobiography, which was notable as the first British book to portray Gordon in an unflattering manner, through Lord Cromer also tried to be fair and emphasised what he felt were Gordon's positive, as well as his negative, traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto40_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto40-239">[239]</a></sup> About the charge that if only Gladstone had listened to Gordon the disaster would have been avoided, Cromer wrote that in the course of one month, he received five telegrams from Gordon offering his advice, each one of which completely contradicted the previous telegram, leading Cromer to charge that Gordon was too mercurial a figure to hold command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> As a young man, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> shared in the national consensus that Gordon was one of Britain's greatest heroes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto39_218-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto39-218">[218]</a></sup> During a meeting in 1898 in Cairo where Churchill interviewed Baring to gather material for his 1899 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_River_War" title="The River War">The River War</a></i>, Baring challenged Churchill about his belief that Gordon was a hero. After his conversation with Baring, Churchill wrote: "Of course there is no doubt that Gordon as a political figure was absolutely hopeless. He was so erratic, capricious, utterly unreliable, his mood changed so often, his temper was abominable, he was frequently drunk, and yet with all that he had a tremendous sense of honour and great abilities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto39_218-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto39-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many biographies have been written of Gordon, most of them of a highly <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographic</a> nature. The British Sinologist <a href="/wiki/Demetrius_Charles_Boulger" title="Demetrius Charles Boulger">Demetrius Charles Boulger</a> published a biography of Gordon in 1896 which depicted him as a staunch patriot and a Christian of immense virtue who displayed superhuman courage in the face of danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> By contrast, Gordon is one of the four subjects discussed critically in <i><a href="/wiki/Eminent_Victorians" title="Eminent Victorians">Eminent Victorians</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Strachey" title="Lytton Strachey">Lytton Strachey</a>, one of the first texts about Gordon that portrays some of his characteristics which Strachey regards as weaknesses. Notably, Strachey emphasizes the claims of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Chaill%C3%A9-Long" title="Charles Chaillé-Long">Charles Chaillé-Long</a> that Gordon was an alcoholic, an accusation dismissed by later writers like <a href="/wiki/Alan_Moorehead" title="Alan Moorehead">Alan Moorehead</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> and Charles Chenevix Trench.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> Strachey depicted Gordon as a ludicrous figure, a bad-tempered, deranged egomaniac with a nasty habit of punching out Arabs whenever he was unhappy who led himself into disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto41_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto41-244">[244]</a></sup> Even more devastatingly, Strachey depicted Gordon as a monumental hypocrite, noting the contrast between Gordon's lofty Christian ideas of love, compassion, charity, grace and hope vs. a career full of hate, war, carnage, death and destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto41_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto41-244">[244]</a></sup> Strachey ended his essay on Gordon on a cynical note: "At any rate, it all ended very happily-in a glorious slaughter of twenty thousand Arabs, a vast addition to the British Empire and a step in the Peerage for Sir Evelyn Baring".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto40_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto40-239">[239]</a></sup> Long after his death and despite the popularity of Strachey's essay in <i>Eminent Victorians</i>, the appeal of the Gordon legend lived on. As late as 1933, the French historian <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Crabit%C3%A8s" title="Pierre Crabitès">Pierre Crabitès</a> wrote in his book <i>Gordon, le Soudan et l'esclavage</i> (<i>Gordon, the Sudan and Slavery</i>) that as a Frenchman the Gordon legend had meant nothing to him when he began researching his book, but after examining all of the historical evidence, he could not help but admire Gordon who "died as he lived, a Christian, a gentleman and a soldier".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto40_239-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto40-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 20th century, many British military leaders came to have a critical view of Gordon with Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Montgomery" title="Bernard Montgomery">Bernard Montgomery</a> writing that Gordon was "unfit for independent command, mentally unbalanced, a fanatic, self-imposed martyr", adding that he should never been sent to the Sudan and the Gladstone-Gordon relationship was a case study in dysfunctional civil-military relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> In 1953, the British novelist Charles Beatty published a Gordon biography <i>His Country was the World, A Study of Gordon of Khartoum</i>, which focused on Gordon's religious faith, but for the first time noted what a tormented figure Gordon was; a man of deeply felt Christian convictions, full of guilt and self-loathing over his own sinfulness and inability to live up to his own impossibly high standards over what a Christian should be and desperately longing to do something to expiate his sinfulness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto42_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto42-246">[246]</a></sup> Like Strachey, Beatty found Gordon a ridiculous figure, but unlike Strachey who had nothing but contempt for Gordon, Beatty's approach was a compassionate one, arguing that Gordon's many acts of charity and self-sacrifice were attempts to love others since he was unable to love himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto42_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto42-246">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another attempt to debunk Gordon was <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Nutting" title="Anthony Nutting">Anthony Nutting</a>'s <i>Gordon, Martyr &amp; Misfit</i> (1966). Nutting's book was noteworthy as the first book to argue that Gordon had a death wish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto43_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto43-247">[247]</a></sup> Nutting noted that Gordon had often recklessly exposed himself to Russian fire while fighting in the Crimea and stated he hoped to die in battle against the Russians before leaving for the Crimea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto43_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto43-247">[247]</a></sup> On the basis of such statements and actions, Nutting argued that Gordon's suicidal courage of going into battle armed only with his rattan cane, which so impressed the Victorian public reflected darker desires. Nutting made the controversial claim that the basis of Gordon's death wish was that he was gay, noting that Gordon never married, is not known to had a relationship with any women, and often wished that he been born a eunuch, which strongly suggested that Gordon wished to have no sexual desires at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto43_247-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto43-247">[247]</a></sup> Nutting contended that the conflict between Gordon's devoutly held Christian ideals and his sexuality made Gordon deeply ashamed of himself and he attempted to expiate his wretched, sinful nature by seeking a glorious death in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto43_247-3"><a href="#cite_note-auto43-247">[247]</a></sup> Behrman wrote that the first part of Nutting's thesis, that Gordon had a death wish is generally accepted by historians, but the second part, that Gordon was a homosexual is still the subject of much debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto43_247-4"><a href="#cite_note-auto43-247">[247]</a></sup> In his <i>Mission to Khartum—The Apotheosis of General Gordon</i> (1969) John Marlowe portrays Gordon as "a colourful eccentric—a soldier of fortune, a skilled guerrilla leader, a religious crank, a minor philanthropist, a gadfly buzzing about on the outskirts of public life" who would have been no more than a footnote in today's history books, had it not been for "his mission to Khartoum and the manner of his death," which were elevated by the media "into a kind of contemporary Passion Play."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup></p>
<p>More balanced biographies are <i>Charley Gordon—An Eminent Victorian Reassessed</i> (1978) by Charles Chenevix Trench and <i>Gordon—the Man Behind the Legend</i> (1988) by John Pollock. In <i>Khartoum—The Ultimate Imperial Adventure</i> (2005), <a href="/wiki/Michael_Asher_(explorer)" title="Michael Asher (explorer)">Michael Asher</a> puts Gordon's works in the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> in a broad context. Asher concludes: "He did not save the country from invasion or disaster, but among the British heroes of all ages, there is perhaps no other who stands out so prominently as an individualist, a man ready to die for his principles. Here was one man among men who did not do what he was told, but what he believed to be right. In a world moving inexorably towards conformity, it would be well to remember Gordon of Khartoum."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the People's Republic of China, the entire period between 1839 and 1949 is depicted as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Century_of_Humiliation" title="Century of Humiliation">Century of Humiliation</a>" – a time when racist, greedy and evil foreigners humiliated and rapaciously exploited the Chinese people. Because many aspects of the Taiping ideology resembled Communism, the Taipings are treated sympathetically by Chinese historians who portrayed as them as prototypical Communists with <a href="/wiki/Hong_Xiuquan" title="Hong Xiuquan">Hong Xiuquan</a> being depicted as anticipating Mao. In this context, Gordon is vilified in China today as just another foreigner oppressing the Chinese people by crushing the Taiping rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> Furthermore, Gordon worked for the Qing dynasty, who were Manchus, which has led many Han to see the entire Qing period between 1644 and 1912 as a long foreign occupation of China. No monuments to Gordon exist in China today, through the British journalist Rob Stallard noted that the modest Gordon would have no doubt wanted it that way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-6"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> Stallard in a 2008 article argued that Gordon deserves a better reputation in China, arguing that he was largely immune to the racist views so common to Westerners in the 19th century and he always treated the Chinese with respect, maintaining that the memory of "Chinese Gordon" could be a bridge to better Anglo-Chinese understanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stallard_38-7"><a href="#cite_note-stallard-38">[38]</a></sup> In Sudan, it is the Mahdi who is the hero and Gordon who is the villain. The Canadian historian C. Brad Faught wrote it was "no coincidence" that the Sudan, a place known as a hotbed of fundamentalist Islam produced both the Mahdi and the current regime of General <a href="/wiki/Omar_al-Bashir" title="Omar al-Bashir">Omar al-Bashir</a>, which venerates the memory of the Mahdi and which for a time in the 1990s sheltered <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto74_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto74-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_George_Gordon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
